**The Message**  
*By Honoré De Balzac*  
*Translated by Ellen Marriage*  

In this narrative, the author expresses a desire to share a poignant story that evokes fear and love among young lovers, compelling them to seek solace in each other. The tale is rooted in personal experience, blending reality with poetic potential.

In 1819, while traveling from Paris to Moulins, the narrator finds himself on the outside of a coach due to financial constraints. He soon strikes up a friendship with a fellow traveler, a young man of better means, and they bond over discussions of love and women, particularly those of a certain age—between thirty-five and forty. Their conversation is filled with youthful exuberance and romantic ideals, leading them to share intimate details about their respective mistresses, both of whom are older than they are.

As they exchange stories of their romantic escapades, the young men reveal their admiration for their mistresses, who are charming and devoted, despite being married. Their camaraderie deepens as they recount their adventures, including the risks they took to be with their lovers. However, their journey takes a tragic turn when the coach overturns, resulting in the death of the narrator's companion.

In his final moments, the young man, crushed beneath the coach, entrusts the narrator with a solemn task: to inform his mistress of his death. He provides the narrator with a key that will grant him access to his home and the letters he wishes to return to her. The young man dies, leaving the narrator with the heavy burden of delivering the news.

Upon reaching La Charité, the narrator fulfills his friend's last wishes, encountering grief from the household staff but focusing on the sorrow awaiting the countess. With limited funds, he decides to walk the remaining distance to the Countess de Montpersan's chateau, driven by a sense of urgency to deliver the tragic news.

As he approaches the chateau, he grapples with the emotional weight of his task, realizing the cruelty of being the bearer of such devastating news. Upon arrival, he finds the chateau open and bustling, but struggles to locate the countess. A young girl assists him, leading him through the park to find her mother.

This story captures the complexities of love, loss, and the burdens of communication, illustrating how a simple journey can transform into a profound encounter with fate. The narrator's reflections on youth, romance, and the inevitability of tragedy resonate throughout, emphasizing the delicate balance between joy and sorrow in human relationships.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
I confess that I took great care to present myself well before meeting Juliette and her husband, M. le Comte and Mme. la Comtesse de Montpersan. I wanted to avoid being mistaken for a local rate collector, and as I reflect on that moment, I find it amusing.

As I composed myself, I spotted Juliette with her husband. She seemed slightly hurried, and upon noticing me, her expression revealed disappointment. I struggled to recall the elegant phrases I had prepared. This hesitation allowed her husband to approach, and I awkwardly inquired if they were indeed the Count and Countess. Observing them closely, I noted the husband’s pompous demeanor and shabby appearance, which contrasted sharply with the Countess's delicate beauty and grace. She was petite, charming, and exuded youthfulness, evoking a strong desire within me.

Realizing the situation, I decided to tactfully inform the Count of my purpose. I discreetly mentioned the death of my traveling companion, which visibly affected him. He expressed concern for his wife, and I assured him that I would not disclose the message I carried until he was informed. This earned me his trust, and we returned to the house where I was invited to dinner.

As we approached, Juliette cast a curious glance at us, revealing her mixed feelings about my unexpected presence. I felt a pang of sympathy for her, recalling her beauty in the sunlight. I then revealed that I had come on behalf of someone who called her Juliette, which startled her. When I mentioned her companion's illness, she grew anxious, fearing for his life. Her desperate plea for the truth struck me deeply, and I could only nod in response.

Just then, the Count called for dinner, and I followed him inside. The meal was lavish, but the Countess was notably absent. As we dined, a maid reported that she could not be found, prompting concern. The canon and I searched the grounds, calling for her, but to no avail. Eventually, we discovered her hidden in a hayloft, sobbing in despair. With the maid's help, we brought her to her room, instructing that she not be disturbed.

Upon returning to the dining room, I found the Count surprisingly unfazed, having consumed most of the meal. His indifference stemmed from a severe dietary restriction due to a medical condition, which explained his lack of concern for his wife’s absence. The evening was somber, filled with unanswered questions about Juliette's distress.

Later that night, I was awakened by the Countess, who appeared at my bedside, pale and shaken. She sought confirmation of the tragic news, and I recounted the accident, careful to spare her the most harrowing details. She listened intently, her grief palpable. When I revealed her companion's fears and the message he entrusted to me, her composure faltered, and she expressed regret for having burned his letters, feeling she had lost everything.

In a moment of connection, I handed her a lock of his hair, a tangible reminder of their bond. Her gratitude was overwhelming, and she wished me happiness in love, before retreating with her precious token.

The following day felt surreal as I reflected on the previous night. I spent time with Juliette, witnessing her noble spirit and selflessness. In the evening, the Count accompanied me partway to Moulins, asking me to deliver a sum of money to a contact in Paris. I willingly accepted, unaware of the thoughtful gesture behind her request. 

This experience deepened my understanding of love and sacrifice, leaving me with a poignant story to share with a woman who cherished me, reminding me of the fragility of life and the bonds we form.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

**GOBSECK**  
*By Honoré De Balzac*  
*Translated By Ellen Marriage*  

**DEDICATION**  
To M. le Baron Barchou de Penhoen.  

In the winter of 1829-30, at one o'clock in the morning, the Vicomtesse de Grandlieu's salon was nearly empty. After a young man departed, the Vicomtesse approached her daughter, Camille, who was distractedly listening to the sounds outside. Concerned about Camille's behavior towards the Comte de Restaud, the Vicomtesse warned her about his mother, Mlle. Goriot, whose questionable past could jeopardize Camille's future. Despite the young Count's admirable qualities, his family background raised alarms.

Derville, a family friend and attorney, overheard their conversation and decided to intervene. He had previously helped the Vicomtesse regain her family's lost properties, significantly improving her financial situation. His success earned him respect in high society, yet he remained humble and declined offers to advance his career further.

As Derville recounted a story that might alter Camille's perception of Restaud, he noted her growing affection for the young man. At a recent ball, Camille expressed confidence in Restaud's potential, believing he would achieve wealth and status through his abilities. Derville, however, pointed out that if Restaud were already wealthy, many girls would compete for his attention, hinting at Camille's feelings for him.

This realization shifted Camille's demeanor towards Derville, who had previously been more of a polite acquaintance than a friend. Her newfound attentiveness suggested a budding connection, despite the underlying tension of social obligations and gratitude that often complicates relationships between different social classes.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Derville reflects on a significant romantic event from his past, centering on a usurer named Gobseck. Gobseck is described as a sallow, impassive man with a meticulous lifestyle, living in a damp, dark house that resembles a convent. He is a solitary figure, rarely interacting with others except for Derville, who becomes his confidant over four years. Gobseck, born Jean-Esther Van Gobseck, is a wealthy usurer who has lived a life filled with adventure and danger, yet remains emotionally detached.

Derville recounts a conversation with Gobseck, who expresses his views on life, emphasizing the importance of gold as the ultimate form of power and reality. He dismisses societal norms and conventions, arguing that self-interest is the only constant in human nature. Gobseck's perspective is cynical; he believes that true happiness lies in the pursuit of wealth and the manipulation of others.

One day, Gobseck shares his experiences collecting debts, revealing two bills of exchange he must collect: one from a Countess and another from a struggling linen-draper named Fanny Malvaut. The Countess, who is beautiful yet burdened by her extravagant lifestyle, ultimately offers Gobseck a diamond in lieu of payment. Derville is captivated by her beauty and the contrast between her life and that of Fanny, who lives modestly and works hard.

Derville visits Fanny, who embodies innocence and sincerity, contrasting sharply with the Countess's world of luxury and moral decay. He contemplates the differences between their lives, feeling a sense of pity for both women. Gobseck's insights into human nature and the power dynamics of society leave Derville feeling disturbed and reflective about the nature of wealth and desire.

As Derville grapples with his feelings for the Countess and his admiration for Fanny, he realizes the profound impact of Gobseck's worldview. The usurer's life, driven by the pursuit of gold and power, serves as a stark reminder of the moral complexities and emotional voids that accompany such a lifestyle. Ultimately, Derville is left contemplating the true cost of wealth and the fleeting nature of beauty and happiness.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
“Will you take a glass of _eau sucree_?” asked the Vicomtesse, interrupting Derville.

“I should be glad of it,” he replied.

Mme. de Grandlieu expressed skepticism about the relevance of the conversation, prompting Derville to invoke Sardanapalus. He recounted how Mademoiselle Camille's happiness had once depended on Daddy Gobseck, who had recently passed away, leaving M. de Restaud a fortune. Derville then mentioned his wife, Fanny Malvaut.

“I would proclaim it to the universe,” he said, affirming his love for her.

Derville continued, explaining that after submitting his thesis, he became a licentiate in law and later an advocate. Gobseck, who had previously underestimated him, began to consult him on complex matters, showing newfound respect. Eventually, Derville became head-clerk and moved into his employer’s house, marking a significant step in his career.

When he bid farewell to Gobseck, the old man showed no emotion but later sought Derville’s advice on a challenging expropriation case. Derville’s principal, a man of pleasure, fell into financial trouble and decided to sell his practice for 150,000 francs. Lacking funds, Derville approached Gobseck for a loan, determined to maintain his self-respect.

Gobseck, after assessing Derville’s situation, agreed to lend him the money at a rate of thirteen percent per annum, a deal Derville accepted despite the high interest. They discussed guarantees, and Gobseck insisted on receiving bills made payable to a third party. Derville agreed to provide legal advice to Gobseck for free as part of their arrangement.

Three months later, Derville became an attorney and successfully recovered estates for Mme. de Grandlieu, gaining recognition in the legal field. He married Fanny, whose inheritance helped him pay off Gobseck within five years. Their shared struggles deepened their bond, leading to a happy life together.

A year after acquiring his practice, Derville attended a bachelor breakfast hosted by M. de Trailles, a well-known dandy. The event was marked by extravagant displays and chaotic revelry, where Derville found himself charmed by de Trailles, who persuaded him to introduce him to Gobseck.

The next day, Derville hesitated but ultimately agreed to accompany de Trailles to meet Gobseck. Upon arrival, de Trailles appeared anxious, revealing a woman in a cab nearby, which excited him. Derville introduced de Trailles to Gobseck, who remained impassive despite de Trailles’ attempts at charm.

Gobseck refused to lend money to de Trailles, citing his debts and lack of security. De Trailles, undeterred, left the room to fetch something he believed would satisfy Gobseck. Gobseck expressed concern about de Trailles’ intentions, revealing a rare moment of vulnerability.

When de Trailles returned with a woman, Derville recognized her as the Countess, one of Goriot’s daughters. The Countess, visibly distressed, sought to pawn her diamonds for cash, hoping to retain the right to repurchase them. Derville explained the terms of the transaction, which relieved her but darkened de Trailles’ mood.

Gobseck examined the diamonds with an unsettling eagerness, revealing his obsession with wealth. His reaction was childlike, contrasting sharply with his usual stoicism. The scene underscored the complex interplay of desire, power, and desperation among the characters, particularly as they navigated the treacherous waters of social and financial relationships in Paris.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The dialogue begins with a discussion about a set of diamonds, valued highly before the Revolution, but now losing worth due to market saturation from Brazilian stones. Gobseck, a moneylender, examines the diamonds with delight, noting their flaws and qualities. The Count seeks a loan of one hundred thousand francs for three years, and Gobseck agrees but insists on weighing the diamonds first. The Countess appears distressed, hinting at her moral struggle.

As Gobseck weighs the diamonds, the narrator observes the Countess's turmoil, sensing her remorse. The narrator questions the Countess about the ownership of the diamonds, which she confirms are hers. However, he refuses to draft the loan agreement, citing legal issues since she is married. Gobseck then offers a deal: eighty thousand francs down, with the diamonds left as collateral. The Countess hesitates, and Maxime, a young man present, expresses his despair, suggesting he will end his troubles soon.

Ultimately, the Countess accepts Gobseck's offer, and he provides her with a check and cash. Tensions rise as Maxime insults Gobseck, who coolly threatens him with pistols. After the Countess leaves, Gobseck revels in his acquisition of the diamonds, showcasing his greed and delight in the transaction.

Days later, the Count visits the narrator, expressing confidence in him and discussing Gobseck's character. The Count reveals his intention to transfer his property to Gobseck, fearing for his health and future. The narrator warns the Count about disinheriting his other children, leading the Count to agree to include them in the counter-deed.

The narrative shifts to the Countess, who, despite her past, is determined to secure her children's future. She becomes increasingly desperate as her husband’s health declines. The Count, bedridden and resentful, forbids her from entering his room, leading her to spy on him. She believes he is hiding money and documents that could secure their wealth.

As the Count nears death, he confides in his son Ernest, entrusting him with a secret letter for the narrator. The Countess, sensing her husband's intentions, tries to manipulate Ernest into revealing the secret. The Count confronts her, leading to a dramatic scene where he accuses her of treachery and expresses his disdain for her actions.

After the Count's death, chaos ensues as the Countess searches for documents, revealing her desperation. The narrator discovers that the Count had hidden important papers, including the counter-deed, which the Countess inadvertently destroys in her panic. Gobseck, now the rightful owner of the property, contemplates the implications of the Countess's actions.

In the aftermath, the narrator reflects on the Countess's transformation and the consequences of her greed. Gobseck, despite his avarice, shows a peculiar understanding of human nature, believing that adversity teaches valuable lessons. The story concludes with the narrator contemplating the fate of the Countess and her children, as well as Gobseck's legacy.

The text explores themes of greed, morality, and the complexities of human relationships, illustrating the destructive power of ambition and the consequences of choices made in desperation.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

**The Marriage Contract: Summary**

**Dedication**  
To Rossini.

**Chapter I: Pro and Con**

Monsieur de Manerville, a respected Norman gentleman, married a wealthy heiress from Bordeaux and became a Gascon after selling his estate. He served as major of the Gate Guards until his death in 1813, having avoided the Revolution by going to Martinique. Upon his return, he found his estate well-managed by Mathias, a notary’s clerk who embraced new ideas. After Madame de Manerville's death in 1810, Monsieur de Manerville became increasingly miserly, depriving his only son, Paul, of resources.

Paul de Manerville, returning from college in 1810, lived under his father's strict control for three years. This oppressive environment stunted his emotional growth, making him timid and unable to assert himself. He felt trapped in his father's old mansion, unable to socialize with peers due to financial constraints. His father took him to royalist gatherings, where Paul felt out of place among the aging nobility. To cope, he engaged in physical activities like fencing and horseback riding, while his intellectual pursuits were limited to novels.

After his father's death, Paul inherited a substantial fortune and estates but was disillusioned with Bordeaux and Lanstrac. He spent six years traveling Europe, eventually exhausting his capital and relying on his estate's revenues. Realizing he needed to settle down, Paul decided to return to Bordeaux, manage his affairs, and marry, believing that his noble title would attract a suitable partner.

Paul, now a count, recognized the value of marriage in society. He had gained worldly knowledge through his experiences, which he believed would enhance his prospects. Despite his extravagant lifestyle, he maintained a reputation for integrity and elegance, avoiding the pitfalls of gambling and deceit.

One morning, Paul confided in his friend de Marsay about his desire to marry and live as a country gentleman. De Marsay, however, warned him against marriage, arguing that it would lead to a loss of freedom and happiness. He criticized the institution, suggesting that marriage was a burden primarily benefiting children, and questioned whether Paul truly wanted to take on such responsibilities.

De Marsay painted a bleak picture of marriage, emphasizing the societal expectations and potential for ridicule that come with it. He advised Paul to enjoy his bachelorhood and consider marrying later in life, perhaps a widow, to avoid the complications of a young bride.

Paul defended his desire for a family, expressing a longing for companionship and stability. He envisioned a life where he could share his joys and secrets with a wife, rejecting de Marsay's cynical view of marriage. He believed he had the courage to be a good husband and father, seeking a deep connection with a partner.

De Marsay remained skeptical, arguing that Paul’s romanticized view of marriage was naive. He warned that the dynamics of love and attraction change once a couple is married, often leading to disappointment. He cautioned Paul about the legal and emotional complexities of marriage, suggesting that a husband must exert control over his wife, which Paul might not be capable of.

Despite de Marsay's warnings, Paul remained resolute in his desire for a family, dismissing the notion that he would become a mere shadow of himself in marriage. He expressed a yearning for a life filled with emotional connections, rather than the superficiality he associated with bachelorhood.

De Marsay continued to challenge Paul’s perspective, arguing that the realities of marriage often clash with the ideals of love. He suggested that Paul’s experiences with women might lead to misconceptions about marital relationships, where the initial excitement can fade into disillusionment.

Ultimately, Paul’s determination to marry stemmed from a desire for genuine companionship and a stable family life, contrasting sharply with de Marsay’s cynical outlook on marriage. The conversation highlighted the tension between societal expectations and personal desires, as Paul sought to navigate the complexities of love and commitment in a changing world.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER II. THE PINK OF FASHION**

Despite the impact of recent conversations, Comte de Manerville returned to Bordeaux in the winter of 1821, determined to restore his family mansion and maintain his reputation for elegance. His connections within the royalist society of Bordeaux quickly elevated him to a position of fashionable prominence, earning him the nickname “the pink of fashion.” His charm, manners, and Parisian sophistication captivated the local elite, and he navigated social interactions with a blend of respect and ease.

Paul de Manerville’s success in provincial society was marked by his attention to personal appearance and a gentle demeanor, which endeared him to both men and women. However, he soon realized that marriage was essential for a fulfilling life in the provinces. Although he was too young to engage in the mundane pursuits of provincial life, he felt a deep longing for companionship, particularly for Mademoiselle Evangelista, the celebrated beauty of Bordeaux.

Mademoiselle Evangelista was the daughter of a wealthy Spanish merchant who had established himself in Bordeaux. After her father's death, Madame Evangelista maintained a lavish lifestyle, raising her daughter in luxury. By the time Paul returned, Natalie was a stunning young woman, yet rumors about her extravagant upbringing made potential suitors hesitant. The prevailing sentiment was that only a prince could afford to marry her, which further complicated her prospects.

When Paul and Natalie first met, they were immediately drawn to each other, each secretly envisioning a future together. Paul was aware of the Evangelista wealth and felt no need for inquiries that would typically deter a suitor. He dismissed the warnings of others regarding the family's extravagance, confident in his ability to manage the financial implications of marrying Natalie.

As Paul frequented the Evangelista household, he became increasingly enchanted by both mother and daughter. Madame Evangelista, at forty, was still strikingly beautiful and possessed a commanding presence. She was adept at navigating social circles and had ambitions for her daughter to rise in Parisian society. Paul, appreciating the warmth and charm of the Evangelistas, found himself spending more time with them, leading society to speculate about his intentions toward Natalie.

Despite the growing attention, neither Paul nor Natalie openly acknowledged their feelings. Natalie maintained a reserved demeanor, which intrigued Paul, while Madame Evangelista subtly encouraged their connection. Paul began to see marriage as a means to secure his social standing and fulfill his ambitions, believing that wealth would mitigate the challenges of married life.

As their relationship developed, Paul idealized Natalie, overlooking potential flaws in her character. He was captivated by her beauty and charm, failing to recognize the complexities that might arise in their future together. The societal pressures and expectations surrounding their union added to the allure, as both families seemed to endorse the match.

However, beneath the surface, Natalie’s upbringing and her mother’s ambitions posed potential challenges. While Paul was drawn to the idea of a life with Natalie, he remained unaware of the deeper dynamics at play, including the influence of Madame Evangelista’s past and her desire for power through her daughter's marriage.

As Paul contemplated the prospect of marriage, he grappled with the realities of commitment and the potential for conflict. He was caught between his romantic ideals and the practicalities of a life intertwined with Natalie and her mother. The narrative foreshadows the complexities of their impending union, highlighting the societal expectations and personal ambitions that would shape their lives.

Ultimately, the chapter sets the stage for the exploration of marriage as a social contract, revealing the intricate dance of desires, ambitions, and the often-unseen challenges that accompany the pursuit of love and status in a society governed by appearances and expectations.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER III. THE MARRIAGE CONTRACT--FIRST DAY**

In the winter of 1822, Paul de Manerville sought the hand of Mademoiselle Natalie Evangelista through his great-aunt, Baronne de Maulincour. The baroness stayed longer than usual in Medoc to assist Paul in this endeavor. After discussing the matter with Madame Evangelista, she returned to inform Paul that the marriage was likely to succeed, but warned him about the dowry situation: Natalie’s fortune was tied to her father’s patrimony, and her mother would not contribute anything of her own.

The baroness advised Paul to consult their notary, Maitre Mathias, to ensure the marriage contract was properly drawn up, emphasizing the importance of protecting his interests for the sake of future children. This left Paul anxious and confused about the implications of the marriage contract and whether he needed to defend his interests against potential deceit from his future mother-in-law.

Following his aunt's advice, Paul approached Madame Evangelista to discuss the marriage contract. She suggested that both families’ notaries should meet to negotiate the terms. Paul, feeling nervous, agreed, relieved that the notaries would handle the details.

Madame Evangelista, however, was under pressure as she owed her daughter a significant sum from her late husband’s estate, which she could not pay. She feared that if Paul learned the truth, he might withdraw his proposal, jeopardizing Natalie’s future. This led her to resolve to manipulate the situation to her advantage.

The next day, she summoned her notary, Maitre Solonet, who was ambitious and eager to please. He assured her that they could navigate the marriage negotiations favorably. Madame Evangelista expressed her desire to maintain control over her daughter’s fortune while ensuring a good match for Natalie.

As the evening approached, both notaries met to discuss the marriage contract. Solonet proposed terms that would benefit Madame Evangelista while Mathias insisted on protecting Paul’s interests. The discussions revealed the stark differences in their approaches: Mathias represented traditional values, while Solonet embodied modern, opportunistic strategies.

During the dinner, Paul and Natalie shared tender moments, but the underlying tension regarding the marriage contract loomed. Paul was enamored with Natalie, but Mathias warned him about the financial implications of marrying into a family that had already squandered much of its wealth.

As the negotiations continued, Mathias expressed concern over the potential ruin of Paul’s fortune if he accepted unfavorable terms. He urged Paul to be cautious, but Paul, blinded by love, was eager to proceed with the marriage.

Madame Evangelista, sensing the precariousness of her position, attempted to reassure Paul of her intentions. However, the discussions became heated, revealing the underlying tensions and distrust between the parties involved. 

Ultimately, a compromise was reached where Madame Evangelista would secure her daughter’s inheritance while also ensuring her own financial stability. The notaries agreed on terms that would allow for the creation of an entail on the family estate, which would protect Paul’s interests and provide for future generations.

As the evening concluded, both families felt a sense of relief, believing they had successfully navigated the complexities of the marriage contract. However, the seeds of distrust had been sown, foreshadowing potential conflicts in the future. 

In the end, all parties left the negotiations feeling satisfied, but the underlying tensions hinted at the challenges that lay ahead in their intertwined lives.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER IV. THE MARRIAGE CONTRACT--SECOND DAY**

The following day, Elie Magus, a jeweler in Bordeaux, was summoned by Madame Evangelista, expecting to discuss jewelry for the upcoming marriage of Comte Paul and Mademoiselle Natalie. Instead, he was asked to appraise the value of her mother’s property, including her diamonds, which were integral to the marriage contract. Magus valued Madame Evangelista's necklace and earrings at 250,000 francs and the “Discreto” diamond at 100,000 francs. However, he offered only 75,000 francs for the diamond and 160,000 for the jewelry if sold, explaining that the value of diamonds decreases when they are owned rather than sold.

This conversation sparked rumors among the household staff and the wider community, leading to speculation about the marriage's stability. Gossip suggested that Madame Evangelista was selling her house and that the marriage might not happen. Despite the notaries denying these claims, public opinion remained skeptical, fueled by jealousy from those who envied the Evangelista family's wealth and status.

As the rumors spread, Paul organized a hunting party, which further fueled speculation about the marriage's future. Madame de Gyas, eager to confirm the gossip, visited Natalie and her mother, feigning concern while relaying the circulating rumors. Madame Evangelista, aware of the motivations behind the gossip, dismissed the concerns, asserting that the marriage contract was a private matter.

In the lead-up to the wedding, Madame Evangelista planned an extravagant celebration called the "fete of the camellias," intending to showcase her wealth and counter the rumors. Preparations took over a month, during which the legalities of the marriage contract were finalized, and the community buzzed with anticipation.

On the day of the contract signing, Madame Evangelista experienced a moment of realization regarding the implications of the contract. She recalled her earlier discussions with the notaries and feared that her daughter's fortune might be compromised. Despite her initial confidence, she resolved to demand clarity on the contract's terms.

The lavish preparations for the fete included transforming the courtyard into a tent filled with camellias, creating an atmosphere of opulence. As guests arrived, the excitement heightened, but the signing of the contract loomed ominously. Paul and Natalie, blissfully unaware of the underlying tensions, engaged in playful banter while the notaries read the contract details.

Madame Evangelista listened intently, but when the entailment clause was explained, she realized the potential financial ruin for her daughter. The entail would limit Natalie’s inheritance, leaving her vulnerable should Paul die first. This revelation struck Madame Evangelista deeply, leading her to conclude that her daughter was at risk of losing her fortune.

In a moment of despair, she recognized that breaking off the marriage was no longer an option; the public nature of the preparations and the expectations of both families made it impossible. She felt trapped, realizing that her earlier manipulations had backfired, and now her daughter’s future was at stake.

As the contract was finalized, Madame Evangelista's emotions boiled over. She confronted the notaries, expressing her anger and frustration at the situation. Solonet, one of the notaries, suggested that they could delay the signing, but Madame Evangelista felt that doing so would only worsen their reputation.

Despite her internal turmoil, she ultimately decided to proceed with the marriage, vowing to protect her daughter’s interests. In a moment of clarity, she resolved to regain control over her daughter’s fortune, planning to invest wisely and ensure Natalie’s security.

The signing of the contract marked a turning point, with Madame Evangelista determined to navigate the complexities of her new reality. As the festivities began, she felt a mix of triumph and resentment, knowing that her daughter’s happiness was intertwined with her own ambitions and fears. The night unfolded with dancing and celebration, but beneath the surface, tensions simmered, setting the stage for future conflicts.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER V. THE MARRIAGE CONTRACT--THIRD DAY**

Despite the festive atmosphere surrounding his upcoming marriage, Paul found himself troubled by doubts when alone. He questioned whether his mother-in-law, Madame Evangelista, might deceive him, but reassured himself that their financial arrangements were clear and that he would soon marry Natalie. However, he resolved to remain vigilant, considering Mathias's warnings.

Unbeknownst to Paul, his future had shifted dramatically. His mother-in-law, once a supportive figure, was now a potential adversary, contemplating how to withdraw her interests from their shared life. Paul failed to recognize the complexities of Madame Evangelista's Creole nature, which combined intellect, passionate volatility, and a certain indifference. This nature, dormant during her years of marital happiness, was now awakening as she faced the impending loss of her daughter.

The next day, Madame Evangelista sat with Natalie, reminiscing about their harmonious life together. She expressed sorrow over Natalie’s impending marriage, warning her that love could quickly fade if not carefully managed. She advised Natalie to establish her authority as a wife from the start, emphasizing the importance of appearing to obey Paul while subtly maintaining control.

Natalie, initially resistant, was persuaded by her mother’s wisdom. Madame Evangelista cautioned her against the dangers of constant proximity in marriage, suggesting that social engagements would help maintain her allure and keep Paul interested. She stressed that a woman must never fully surrender her power, advising Natalie to cultivate a sense of mystery and reserve.

As they prepared for the wedding, Madame Evangelista revealed her intention to sell her jewelry to support her daughter, proposing an exchange that would benefit Natalie. Paul, moved by his mother-in-law's sacrifice, agreed to the arrangement, unaware of the underlying tensions.

When Paul arrived, he appeared troubled, prompting Madame Evangelista to suspect that Mathias had influenced him. She proposed selling her jewelry to ensure Natalie had valuable pieces, emphasizing the importance of a woman's vanity in maintaining happiness. Paul accepted, and they discussed the sale of their property, with Natalie showing signs of understanding business matters.

Madame Evangelista, determined to step back from their lives, insisted that her presence might hinder their marriage. She expressed a desire to manage her own affairs at Lanstrac, believing that her absence would allow Natalie and Paul to bond without her influence. Paul, relieved by this arrangement, felt a sense of freedom.

As the wedding approached, Natalie felt anxious about her new life without her mother. Despite her fears, she was determined to be a good wife. The ceremony took place at midnight, a time often associated with ominous omens. Natalie, now separated from her mother, was filled with doubt and apprehension about her future.

The guests noted the unusual circumstances of the wedding, with some criticizing Paul for excluding Madame Evangelista. They speculated about the implications of such a decision, suggesting it might invite trouble into their marriage. Meanwhile, Madame Evangelista, having played her part, left Natalie with a final piece of advice: to be a wife, not a mistress.

After the ceremony, she feigned despair to extract promises from Paul, ensuring he would remain committed to Natalie. As they departed for Paris, Natalie signaled to her mother that Paul had won the initial battle, but she was already beginning to assert her influence over him.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER VI. CONCLUSION**

Five years later, in November, Comte Paul de Manerville arrived at the home of his old friend, Monsieur Mathias, in Bordeaux. Mathias, now retired from his notary practice, was absent but his housekeeper led Paul to the room of the late Madame Mathias. Exhausted from his journey, Paul slept until evening. When Mathias returned, he found Paul looking changed but calm. They shared a meal, during which Mathias expressed his sorrow over the public notices of Paul's financial troubles, which he had nurtured for years.

Mathias lamented the loss of Paul's estate, which had been in the family for generations. He was particularly distressed by the public nature of the seizures, feeling personally shamed by the announcements. Paul revealed that he had left his wife, Natalie, without her knowledge, and was sailing to Calcutta to rebuild his fortune under the name Camille. Mathias was shocked at Paul's decision to enter commerce, believing it beneath his dignity.

Paul explained that he had incurred massive debts, largely due to lavish spending and the costs associated with his marriage. He had given Natalie expensive gifts and had to sell her investments to maintain their lifestyle. Mathias was incredulous at the scale of Paul's debts, which amounted to over a million and a half francs. Paul insisted that he could not burden Natalie with their financial troubles and preferred to separate their assets.

Mathias suggested that Paul could live off Natalie's income while recovering, but Paul rejected this idea, preferring to work for his fortune rather than rely on her. He believed that women should not be involved in financial matters. Mathias, however, felt that a wife should share both the joys and burdens of marriage.

The next day, Mathias received a bill of exchange for 150,000 francs from de Marsay, a friend of Paul’s. Despite Mathias's concerns about Paul's plans, he ultimately accepted Paul's determination to leave for India. The ship, "Belle-Amelie," was set to sail, and Mathias accompanied Paul to the quay, where they were met with curiosity and gossip about Paul's downfall.

As Paul prepared to board, Mathias signaled him with a handkerchief, indicating he had important letters. However, the sailor delivering the letters did not convey the urgency of the message. Paul, wanting to avoid being swayed by their contents, pocketed the letters unread.

Once at sea, Paul reflected on his life and the choices that led him to this point. He felt a mix of grief and uncertainty about leaving Natalie behind. Overcome by seasickness, he spent days in his cabin, contemplating his situation. Eventually, he opened the letters from Natalie and de Marsay.

Natalie's letter expressed her anguish over Paul's departure and her desire to support him. She felt betrayed by his decision to leave without consulting her and vowed to economize and pay off his debts. She also revealed that she believed she was pregnant, which filled her with hope for their future.

In contrast, de Marsay's letter detailed the extent of Paul's financial ruin, revealing that Natalie's mother had orchestrated much of the situation. De Marsay warned Paul of the conspiracy against him and urged him to return to Paris instead of going to India. He suggested that Paul could regain his honor by confronting his rivals and reclaiming his position.

As Paul absorbed the harsh realities of his situation, he felt a mix of anger and despair. He questioned what he had done to deserve such betrayal and turmoil. Ultimately, he resolved to confront his challenges head-on, determined to rebuild his life and restore his honor.

---

This summary condenses the original text while retaining the essential plot points and character dynamics. The focus remains on Paul's internal struggles, his relationships with Mathias, Natalie, and de Marsay, and the overarching themes of love, betrayal, and the consequences of one's choices.

**A Start in Life**  
**By Honoré De Balzac**  
**Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley**  

**DEDICATION**  
To Laure.  
Let the brilliant mind that inspired this scene have the honor of it.  
Her brother,  
De Balzac  

---

**CHAPTER I. THAT WHICH WAS LACKING TO PIERROTIN’S HAPPINESS**  

In the near future, railroads will render certain transportation industries obsolete, particularly around Paris. The Touchards, a father-son duo, monopolized travel to towns within a 45-mile radius, but faced fierce competition from local services, particularly the coucous, which thrived on shorter routes. Despite their efforts, the Touchards struggled against smaller competitors who had the advantage of local goodwill and familiarity with their customers.

One of the most contested routes was from Paris to Beaumont-sur-Oise, where the Touchards faced three rivals in 1822. The competition was fierce due to the profitability of the line, which served several towns and villages. The Touchards eventually extended their route to Chambly, but competition persisted.

The road to Isle-Adam, a charming town known for its quarries and historical significance, was less competitive, with only two coaches operating harmoniously. Pierrotin, a well-liked coachman, and his rival shared a good relationship, often coordinating their schedules to benefit their customers. They operated from the same yard in Paris, the “Lion d’Argent,” where the innkeeper’s wife managed bookings and fares.

On a Saturday morning in 1822, Pierrotin stood outside the inn, anxious about the lack of passengers. He had ordered a new coach to expand his business but was short on funds to pay for it. Despite his ambitions, he faced financial strain, having exhausted his resources to secure the new vehicle. He hoped to attract more customers with the new coach, which was designed to rival the Touchards’ offerings.

Pierrotin’s current vehicle, an inherited coucou, was cramped and uncomfortable, often overloaded with passengers. He had plans for a larger, more luxurious coach, but needed to raise a thousand francs to complete the payment. As he contemplated his options, a footman approached him, seeking a ride for his master, the Comte de Serizy, who wished to travel incognito.

The footman revealed that the count had countermanded a carriage for reasons unknown, hinting at potential intrigue. Pierrotin, eager to please and hoping for a generous tip, agreed to the arrangement, despite the footman’s warning to keep the count’s identity secret from other passengers.

As Pierrotin prepared for the journey, he reflected on the count’s steward, Monsieur Moreau, a man he respected and who often employed him for errands. The conversation with the footman left Pierrotin curious about the count’s motives for traveling discreetly, and he considered the implications for Moreau, whom he believed deserved protection.

The chapter sets the stage for a narrative exploring ambition, social dynamics, and the evolving transportation landscape in early 19th-century France, highlighting Pierrotin's struggles and aspirations as he navigates the complexities of his profession and the relationships within it.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER II. THE STEWARD IN DANGER**

Monsieur Huguet de Serisy descends from the notable president Huguet, ennobled under François I. The family coat of arms features a party per pale or and sable, with the motto “i, semper melius eris,” reflecting the modesty of burgher families in earlier times. The current count, a councillor of State at just twenty-two, distinguished himself during the Revolution by remaining on his estate, Serizy, where he was protected by his father's reputation. After his father's death in 1794, he was elected to the Council of the Five Hundred, later becoming a prominent figure under Napoleon, who appointed him to the Council of State and made him a count and senator.

In 1806, he married the widow of a Republican general, significantly increasing his wealth. By 1814, due to health issues, he resigned from his posts and returned to Paris, where he maintained a loyal stance towards the Bourbons, refusing Napoleon's peerage during the Hundred Days. After Napoleon's second fall, he resumed his political duties, becoming vice-president of the Council of State and managing foreign claims without seeking personal glory. His life was marked by hard work, rising early and retiring early, earning numerous honors for his service.

Despite his public success, the count suffered privately due to his unfulfilled love for his wife, who enjoyed considerable freedom and social engagement. He respected her autonomy, allowing her to manage her affairs while he remained devoted to her, even as his own health deteriorated. The count's dedication to his work masked his emotional pain, stemming from jealousy and the complexities of their marriage.

The narrative shifts to a pressing matter involving a farm that encroached upon the count's estate, Presles. The lease on this farm, held by a farmer named Leger, was expiring, and the owner, Margueron, refused to renew it. The count sought to purchase the farm to eliminate ongoing disputes. He consulted his notary and lawyer, who advised him to invite Margueron to dinner to finalize the sale. The count planned to bring part of the purchase money and secure a government position for Margueron's son as part of the deal.

Before this meeting, the count had already initiated renovations at Presles, intending to surprise his wife. However, he unexpectedly decided to travel incognito to avoid drawing attention. 

Moreau, the steward of Presles, had a complex background. His father had saved the Serizys during the Revolution, and Moreau had been granted a position by the count after escaping a death sentence. Initially loyal and diligent, Moreau's character changed after he became comfortable in his role, leading him to accept bribes and engage in dishonest dealings. He aimed to profit from the sale of the farm, planning to pocket a significant sum while maintaining the appearance of integrity.

As the count prepared for his trip, Moreau conspired with Leger to inflate the farm's price, hoping to benefit from the transaction. Meanwhile, a retired officer, Monsieur de Reybert, and his wife, feeling wronged by Moreau, sought to expose his dishonesty to the count. Madame de Reybert approached the count, claiming that Moreau was scheming to defraud him in the farm purchase.

Though initially skeptical, the count began to doubt Moreau's integrity after reading a letter from the steward that contradicted his assurances. He decided to investigate further, instructing his notary to accompany Madame de Reybert back to Presles and to delay sending his clerk as previously planned.

Thus, the count's incognito journey and the warning from Madame de Reybert set the stage for a confrontation that could jeopardize Moreau's position and the count's interests.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER III. THE TRAVELLERS**

As Pierrotin left the Café de l’Echiquier, he spotted Madame Clapart and her son Oscar at the Lion d’Argent. The mother, dressed in a worn black silk gown and an old shawl, was anxiously searching for him. Her appearance suggested a once-beautiful woman now burdened by life, devoted to her son, who was traveling alone for the first time. Oscar, in a short olive-green coat and patched blue trousers, reflected their modest means.

“Is this the conductor? Ah! Pierrotin, is it you?” she exclaimed, leaving her son to speak with him privately. She asked Pierrotin to take good care of Oscar, who was heading to Monsieur Moreau’s place. Pierrotin, aware of the challenges ahead for Oscar, hesitated to express his concerns.

Pierrotin often transported goods for Moreau, who had a discreet arrangement with Madame Clapart. He would deliver baskets of food and supplies to her home, which Pierrotin found puzzling given the household's poverty. Madame Clapart lived in a modest apartment, filled with old furniture and signs of financial struggle, yet she maintained a regal demeanor.

Monsieur Clapart, her husband, was a timid man with a tyrannical streak, and their life was marked by hardship. Despite their struggles, Madame Clapart’s love for Oscar was unwavering, though it blinded her to his shortcomings. Oscar, raised in a world of past splendor, was now facing the harsh realities of life, compounded by his stepfather’s disdain for him.

Moreau, who had once been close to Madame Clapart, had helped her secure a scholarship for Oscar at a prestigious school. However, Oscar’s vanity and lack of self-awareness troubled Moreau, who wished to guide him toward a suitable career.

As they prepared for the journey, Madame Clapart fussed over Oscar, reminding him to avoid discussing servants and to be respectful. Oscar, embarrassed by his mother’s attentiveness, longed for independence. He felt humiliated by the presence of two better-dressed young men nearby, who mocked him for his mother’s affection.

The young men, Georges and Amaury, engaged in playful banter, further aggravating Oscar’s insecurities. He envied their confidence and style, feeling trapped by his own circumstances. As the coach was readied, Pierrotin managed the logistics, while the young men continued to tease Oscar, who tried to appear indifferent.

Eventually, the coach was filled with passengers, including a stout farmer named Pere Leger and the Comte de Serizy, who arrived last. The count, disguised as a bourgeois, sought to maintain his incognito amidst the youthful mockery directed at him. 

As the journey began, Georges expressed impatience with Pierrotin’s pace, while Mistigris, a young artist, made light of the situation. The count, overhearing their comments, remained composed, aware of the importance of keeping his identity hidden.

The group’s dynamics shifted as they traveled, with Oscar grappling with feelings of inadequacy and envy, while the others engaged in light-hearted exchanges. The journey promised to be a mix of tension and camaraderie, as each character navigated their own social standings and personal aspirations.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER IV. THE GRANDSON OF THE FAMOUS CZERNI-GEORGES**

In public transport, passengers often remain silent at first, engaging in mutual observation until conversation begins, typically initiated by the most talkative member. This is particularly true in French stage-coaches, where lively banter is common, unlike the more reserved customs of other nations. Georges, a clever and lively young man, quickly assessed his fellow travelers, deeming himself superior and planning to amuse himself at their expense.

As the coach descended from La Chapelle, Georges contemplated various identities he could adopt to entertain himself, ultimately deciding to present himself as a disguised Russian prince. He engaged in playful banter with Mistigris, who was traveling with a count, and the other passengers, weaving tales of his supposed adventures in the East, including serving under Ali, pacha of Janina.

Georges claimed to have been a soldier, embellishing his past with stories of battles and decorations, which intrigued the other passengers. He recounted his experiences in Egypt and Greece, painting a picture of a life filled with excitement and danger. He mentioned his lineage as the grandson of Czerni-Georges, a historical figure who had fought against the Ottoman Empire, and described his return to Greece, where he was welcomed by Ali Tebelen.

As the conversation flowed, Georges boasted about his time in a harem, downplaying its significance and expressing a preference for the company of French women. The group arrived in Saint-Denis, where they stopped at a tavern known for its cheese-cakes. Georges, now enjoying the attention of his fellow travelers, paid for their treats and continued to weave his elaborate tales.

The count, intrigued yet skeptical, suspected that Georges was not who he claimed to be. He discovered a portfolio belonging to Georges, revealing him to be Crottat’s second clerk, which confirmed his suspicions. Meanwhile, Georges continued to impress the others with his stories, even as the count remained wary of his true identity.

As they resumed their journey, Georges shared more about his supposed military exploits, claiming to have played a crucial role in battles and to have received lavish gifts from Ali Tebelen. However, he also recounted the misfortunes that befell him, including losing his treasures to the Turkish admiral after being captured.

The count, concerned about Georges’ reckless boasting, warned him about the consequences of speaking too freely about his past, especially regarding foreign intrigues. Georges, undeterred, continued to entertain the group with tales of his adventures, including a dramatic encounter in Dalmatia involving a beautiful woman named Zena and a jealous pirate husband.

Schinner, a painter traveling with the group, chimed in with his own stories of love and danger, recounting his time in Dalmatia and the complications that arose from his infatuation with Zena. The narrative shifted between Georges and Schinner, both men vying for the attention of their fellow travelers while weaving intricate tales of their pasts.

As the journey progressed, the count and the others listened with a mix of skepticism and fascination, caught up in the drama and excitement of the stories being told. Georges and Schinner’s narratives intertwined, creating a tapestry of adventure, romance, and intrigue that captivated their audience, even as the count remained cautious about the truth behind their tales.

Ultimately, the journey served as a backdrop for the exploration of identity, storytelling, and the allure of adventure, with each character revealing their desires and aspirations through the stories they shared. The interplay of truth and fiction became a central theme, as the travelers navigated their own realities while being drawn into the fantastical worlds created by Georges and Schinner.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER V. THE DRAMA BEGINS**

Pierrotin's coach descended into the village of Saint-Brice, where he typically stopped to rest his horses. It was around 1:30 PM when they arrived at the inn.

“Ah! Here’s Pere Leger,” the innkeeper called out as the coach halted. “Do you breakfast?”

“Always once a day,” replied the farmer, ready to eat.

“Give us a good breakfast!” Georges exclaimed, twirling his cane, which impressed Oscar. However, Oscar's admiration turned to jealousy when Georges lit a cigar from a small case.

“Do you smoke?” Georges asked Oscar.

“Sometimes,” Oscar replied, trying to appear nonchalant.

Georges offered the case to Oscar and Schinner, who noted the cigars were cheap. Schinner declined breakfast, having eaten earlier, while Oscar claimed he had already eaten too. He envied Georges's carefree spending, calculating the costs of their trip.

As they waited, Mistigris joked about their mismatched socks, prompting laughter from the count, who observed the young men with a mix of amusement and envy.

The innkeeper and Pere Leger discussed a potential land deal involving the Comte de Serizy, hinting at a scheme to outmaneuver the nobleman. The count overheard their conversation, suspecting a conspiracy involving his steward, Moreau.

Pierrotin, fearing indiscretion, promised the count to keep his identity secret in exchange for a future payment. He decided not to join the others for breakfast, citing a delivery he needed to make.

Pere Leger suggested they harness a horse to the cabriolet and have breakfast before catching up with Pierrotin. The count, meanwhile, was intrigued by the farmer's plans and the potential profits involved.

As Pierrotin drove away, Georges and the farmer continued discussing the beauty of the landscape, while Oscar, feeling belittled, attempted to assert his future importance in diplomacy. His claims drew laughter from the others, who found his pretensions amusing.

Oscar insisted he was friends with the Comte de Serizy's son, which surprised Pere Leger. The count, intrigued, asked Oscar for information about the count, leading to Oscar revealing personal details about the count's life and health, which shocked the count.

The conversation turned to the countess, and the count interrupted, defending her honor. He then revealed his connections and left the coach at an inn, leaving the young men in a state of confusion over their earlier bravado.

As they approached the chateau de Presles, Pierrotin revealed that he was to drop off the young men at the estate. Oscar, eager to assert his status, rushed ahead, unaware of the path he took. Pere Leger pointed him in the right direction, while Georges slipped away, realizing the farmer's connection to Les Moulineaux.

Pierrotin delivered the packages to the concierge, leaving the young men to contemplate their earlier claims and the reality of their situation. As Pierrotin drove away, he felt satisfied, knowing he would soon receive his payment and a new coach.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER VI. THE MOREAU INTERIOR**

Oscar, feeling shy, hid behind some trees in the courtyard, observing his companions when Monsieur Moreau appeared on the portico, dressed in a long blue overcoat and carrying a riding whip. He greeted Oscar warmly and introduced himself to Mistigris and his master, the two painters sent by architect Monsieur Grindot. Moreau instructed the concierge to take them to their rooms and invited them to dinner at five, mentioning the hunting opportunities available on the estate.

As they walked through the park, Moreau sent his son Jacques to inform Madame Moreau of Oscar's arrival. Moreau, a stern-looking man with a kind nature, encouraged Oscar to learn horseback riding and hunting, despite Oscar's reluctance. He mentioned that Oscar's stay was only supposed to be two weeks due to Madame Moreau's presence.

The steward's house, built before the Revolution, was situated in a large garden and connected to the chateau. It featured a salon, dining room, and a modern boudoir for Madame Moreau, who had decorated it lavishly. The Moreaus lived comfortably, relying on the estate's resources while maintaining an appearance of independence. Madame Moreau, a former maid, enjoyed her status and often dressed elegantly, seeking to be seen as a lady of the chateau rather than a steward's wife.

Tensions existed between the Moreaus and the Reyberts, stemming from a past incident where Madame Reybert reminded the community of Madame Moreau's previous station. The Moreaus had cultivated relationships with local bourgeoisie, often hosting gatherings and playing cards with the rector and mayor.

Madame Moreau eagerly awaited the arrival of the two painters, hoping to impress them and elevate her social standing. She dressed in her finest attire, anticipating their arrival. However, when the coach from Paris arrived, it only delivered Mistigris's packages, leaving her disappointed.

Later, when the artists arrived, Madame Moreau welcomed them and expressed her love for art. She attempted to impress them with her knowledge and charm, but her past as a maid occasionally slipped through her facade. The artists, recognizing her pretensions, decided to play along, engaging her in conversation about art and flattery.

Just as the atmosphere became more relaxed, a maid interrupted, announcing the count's unexpected arrival. This news shocked Madame Moreau, who was concerned about the implications of the count's visit. The count, having overheard Oscar's comments about him during the journey, was furious. He confronted Moreau about the gossip and betrayal, revealing that he felt deeply hurt by the steward's actions.

Moreau, distressed, tried to explain that he had spoken of the count with affection, but the damage was done. The count decided to dismiss Moreau from his position, allowing him to keep his earnings but severing their relationship. He instructed Moreau to ensure a smooth transition to the new steward, Monsieur de Reybert.

As the count prepared for dinner, the atmosphere was tense. The arrival of various guests, including notaries and local dignitaries, added to the pressure. Mistigris, unaware of the gravity of the situation, made light-hearted comments, while Georges Marest, a clerk, found himself in trouble for his earlier antics.

Meanwhile, Moreau returned home, distraught over the loss of his position and the implications for his family. He confronted Oscar, blaming him for the situation and demanding an apology to the count. Oscar, overwhelmed with fear and shame, resisted, leading to a dramatic confrontation where Moreau forced him to apologize in front of the count.

The count, unimpressed by Oscar's behavior, dismissed him, leaving Moreau to grapple with the consequences of the evening's events. The chapter concludes with Moreau taking Oscar home, contemplating the fallout from the count's visit and the future of their family.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER VII. A MOTHER’S TRIALS**

While preparing for a journey, Moreau wrote to Madame Clapart, expressing his distress over Oscar's actions. During a trip, Oscar had carelessly revealed sensitive information about Madame de Serizy to a high-ranking official, leading to Moreau's dismissal as steward of Presles. Moreau was sending Oscar back home immediately, feeling the weight of his family's future and his own ambitions. He lamented Oscar's behavior and questioned his intelligence, wishing he had received better guidance before coming to stay with them.

That evening, Madame Clapart was knitting when her husband, Clapart, expressed concern that their friend Poiret might have visited while they were out. Clapart was critical of Oscar, believing he would cause trouble at Presles. Madame Clapart defended her son, highlighting his good nature and potential. Clapart dismissed her optimism, insisting Oscar was lazy and incapable.

Suddenly, a carriage arrived, returning Oscar home. Brochon, the stable-man, informed them that Moreau had lost his position due to Oscar's actions. Madame Clapart, distressed, fainted upon reading Moreau's letter. When she regained consciousness, she urged Oscar to go to bed, while Clapart continued to berate him.

Despite the turmoil, Oscar slept soundly, unaware of the gravity of his situation. The next day, he felt hungry and surprised that life continued as normal. His mother, worried about their future without Moreau's support, explained to Oscar the dire consequences of his actions. She emphasized the need for him to find a career, outlining the limited options available to him, including commerce, government work, or military service. She expressed her fears about their financial instability and urged him to enlist in the army.

Oscar, still naive about life's challenges, listened to his mother but struggled to grasp the reality of their situation. His mother's tears moved him, and she implored him to promise to be more discreet in the future. They discussed seeking help from Uncle Cardot, who had prospered in business and might assist Oscar.

Uncle Cardot, a wealthy widower, lived modestly and had provided well for his children. He was unaware of the struggles faced by Madame Clapart and Oscar. When they visited him, she highlighted Oscar's achievements, hoping to gain his support. Cardot, while polite, was skeptical about Oscar's future and the family's needs.

Madame Clapart revealed their financial struggles, explaining how she had sacrificed for Oscar's education. Cardot, moved by her plight, offered to help Oscar pursue a legal career, promising to cover his educational costs if he showed promise. He advised Oscar to work hard and maintain integrity, emphasizing the importance of discretion in a big city like Paris.

After their visit, Madame Clapart felt hopeful for Oscar's future. Moreau later visited, having started a new venture in land speculation. He reassured her that Oscar could still have a successful career if he remained disciplined. Moreau proposed that Oscar work under a young lawyer named Desroches, who would provide the structure and guidance he needed.

Madame Clapart was grateful for Moreau's continued support and urged Oscar to express his thanks. Moreau emphasized that the best way for Oscar to redeem himself was through hard work and proper conduct moving forward.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER VIII. TRICKS AND FARCES OF THE EMBRYO LONG ROBE**

Ten days later, Oscar was introduced by Monsieur Moreau to Maitre Desroches, a young solicitor in a modest office on rue de Bethisy. Desroches, who had risen from poverty, took a keen interest in Oscar, recognizing his own past struggles. His stern demeanor, however, intimidated Oscar.

Desroches informed his head clerk, Monsieur Godeschal, that Oscar would be living in the attic next to Godeschal’s room and would need strict supervision to ensure he excelled in law school and learned the Code thoroughly. Godeschal, who had also come from humble beginnings, was tasked with guiding Oscar, emphasizing the importance of hard work and integrity.

For two years, Oscar lived a monk-like existence in the office, waking at five each morning to work alongside Godeschal. He performed errands, prepared for law school, and faced rigorous examinations from both Godeschal and Desroches. His daily routine included a simple dinner and continued work until nightfall. Oscar occasionally visited his uncle Cardot and spent Sundays with his mother, but he was often reprimanded for his attempts at elegance.

Godeschal advised Oscar on maintaining a modest wardrobe and living frugally, citing Desroches as an example of success through hard work. Eighteen months into his tenure, Oscar became the third clerk, performing the duties of the second clerk without a salary but receiving room and board. By the end of his second year, he had gained significant legal knowledge and was performing well at the Palais.

Despite his progress, Oscar struggled internally, feeling trapped in a life that clashed with his desires. He envied the well-dressed young men he saw on the streets and felt a longing for freedom and pleasure, which he suppressed under Godeschal's guidance. Godeschal, aware of Oscar's struggles, occasionally took him out for leisure to help him cope with the monotony of his life.

In November 1825, after Oscar passed his exams, he received funds from Moreau to upgrade his wardrobe, and he began working as the second clerk with a salary of eight hundred francs. His uncle Cardot promised to help him find a practice if he continued to excel.

However, Oscar's internal conflict persisted. He often contemplated leaving his rigorous life, feeling more like a galley slave than a budding lawyer. Godeschal remained vigilant, ensuring Oscar was not exposed to temptations, as he often lacked money for excesses. Godeschal's occasional outings with Oscar provided some relief from his otherwise dreary existence.

When Godeschal returned from a trip, he reported to Moreau that Oscar was becoming vain, dressing too elegantly and seeking social adventures. He worried that Oscar's aspirations might not align with a legal career, despite his talent for conversation.

Shortly after Oscar's promotion, a new clerk named Frederic Marest joined the office. Marest, a wealthy young man aspiring to become a magistrate, sought to learn the intricacies of legal practice. Godeschal announced Marest's arrival, hoping he would contribute to the office's camaraderie.

The clerks engaged in a tradition of welcoming new members with a humorous register filled with fictitious cases and exaggerated accounts of past receptions. This playful spirit was a way for the clerks to bond and relieve the monotony of their work.

As Oscar settled into his role, he participated in the office's lighthearted pranks, including the ongoing joke surrounding the register. When Marest arrived, he quickly caught on to the humor, indicating he was not easily fooled.

In summary, Oscar's journey through the legal world was marked by rigorous discipline, internal conflict, and the camaraderie of his fellow clerks, all while navigating the challenges of ambition and societal expectations.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER IX: LA MARQUISE DE LAS FLORENTINAS Y CABIROLOS**

The next day, Georges Marest, now head-clerk of notary Hannequin, visited Oscar, who recognized him as the friend from a past misadventure. Georges invited Oscar and his colleagues to a lavish gathering at the Rocher de Cancale and later to an evening with Madame la Marquise de las Florentinas y Cabirolos, promising a night of elegance and excitement.

Desroches, Oscar's superior, instructed him to retrieve a crucial legal document, warning him to be cautious of Derville, who might obstruct their efforts. Oscar was eager to prove himself in this first assignment since becoming a second clerk.

After Georges left, Godeschal questioned Frederic about the marquise, who continued the ruse, suggesting she was a wealthy widow from Mexico. The clerks, excited by the prospect of mingling with high society, prepared for the event.

The Marquise de las Florentinas y Cabirolos was actually Mademoiselle Agathe-Florentine Cabirolle, a successful danseuse at the Gaiete, supported by her benefactor, Pere Cardot. Over the years, Florentine had risen in status, enjoying a luxurious lifestyle and courting Georges, who had recently inherited a substantial fortune.

As the clerks anticipated the gathering, Oscar felt a mix of excitement and apprehension, especially regarding Georges, who had previously caused him distress. Despite his reservations, he donned his new clothes and set out to enjoy the day.

At the Rocher de Cancale, Oscar was introduced to various guests, including an old captain and a journalist. Georges charmed Oscar, downplaying past grievances. The feast was extravagant, and the clerks indulged in food and drink, leading to a state of intoxication.

Afterward, they headed to Florentine's salon, where they were greeted by a dazzling array of women and opulence. Oscar was captivated by Florentine's transformation into a sophisticated marquise. He was soon drawn into a game of vingt-et-un with Fanny Beaupre, an actress, and lost significant money, including the five hundred francs entrusted to him by Desroches.

Overwhelmed by his losses, Oscar contemplated despair but was reassured by Florentine, who offered him a thousand francs to recover his losses. He accepted, hoping to regain his standing. However, as the night progressed, he lost even more, succumbing to the thrill of gambling and drink.

By the early morning, Oscar was exhausted and fell asleep on a sofa, oblivious to the ongoing festivities. Florentine and her friends discussed the events, unaware of the trouble Oscar faced due to his reckless gambling. The night ended with Oscar still asleep, caught in the consequences of his choices.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**CHAPTER X. ANOTHER CATASTROPHE**

The next morning, a loud voice woke the clerk, Oscar Husson, who pretended to sleep, recognizing his uncle Cardot. Cardot scolded him for his late-night revelry and the state of the apartment, expressing disappointment that Oscar had not informed him about the gathering. Florentine, a dancer, defended herself, claiming she had invited guests to please Cardot.

As tensions rose, Florentine revealed that Oscar had lost money gambling, prompting Cardot to chastise him for his recklessness. Oscar, distraught, begged Cardot to help him explain the situation to his employer, Monsieur Desroches, as he feared losing his job. Florentine urged Cardot to save Oscar, reminding him of their family ties.

Cardot reluctantly gave Oscar five hundred francs, warning it would be the last time he would help him. Oscar left, unsure of where to go, as he faced the consequences of his actions. Meanwhile, Mariette, concerned for Oscar, sent a note and money to help him recover his losses.

Desroches, upon receiving the note, was furious with Oscar for his irresponsibility. Godeschal, Oscar's friend, defended him, suggesting that Oscar's vanity was leading him astray. Desroches decided to dismiss Oscar, believing he did not deserve further assistance.

At home, Oscar's mother, Madame Clapart, learned of her son's troubles and was devastated. Clapart, her husband, mocked her distress, but she remained hopeful for Oscar's future. When Oscar returned home, he confronted Clapart, asserting his independence and rejecting his father's authority. Clapart's harsh words only deepened Madame Clapart's sorrow.

Oscar, feeling the weight of his mistakes, resolved to change his ways. He drew a military number, which determined his fate. Despite the challenges ahead, he hoped for a better future.

Three days later, Oscar drew number twenty-seven, leading to his enlistment in the cavalry. His fortunes changed when he was assigned to a noble regiment, thanks to the influence of the Comte de Serizy. Madame Clapart, overwhelmed by her son's misfortunes, turned to religion for solace, believing her past sins were the cause of her family's suffering.

Oscar excelled in the military, rising through the ranks and earning honors for his bravery during a battle in Africa. He saved the Vicomte de Serizy, which solidified his reputation and led to further promotions.

**CHAPTER XI. OSCAR’S LAST BLUNDER**

Years later, Oscar, now a decorated officer, traveled with his mother to Beaumont. They encountered Georges Marest, a former acquaintance whose life had taken a downward turn. Georges, unrecognizable and in poor condition, attempted to engage with Oscar, who had changed significantly since their last meeting.

As they traveled, Oscar reflected on the changes in their lives. He had risen from his past mistakes, while Georges had fallen into obscurity. The journey brought back memories of their shared past, highlighting the stark contrast between their current circumstances.

Oscar's military career continued to flourish, and he eventually married Georgette Pierrotin, the daughter of the coach owner. This union marked a new chapter in his life, as he learned from his past blunders and embraced a more responsible and honorable path.

Through his experiences, Oscar matured into a respectable man, earning a position as a collector and later a receiver-general. His journey from a reckless youth to a responsible adult exemplified the lessons learned from his earlier mistakes, ultimately leading to a fulfilling life.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
