Chabert expressed his despair over being treated as a madman and his longing for vengeance against his wife, who had not acknowledged his existence since his return.

Derville, moved by Chabert's plight, offered to help him obtain the necessary documents to prove his identity. He promised to provide financial assistance while they pursued the case. Chabert, overwhelmed with gratitude, recognized Derville as a rare ally in his fight for justice.

As they discussed the legal complexities of Chabert's situation, the Colonel's demeanor shifted from despair to determination. He expressed a willingness to fight for his rights, even if it meant facing significant challenges in court. Derville assured him that he would take the case seriously and would work diligently to help him reclaim his life.

After their meeting, Chabert left with a sense of hope, clutching the money Derville had given him. For the first time in years, he felt a glimmer of possibility in his otherwise bleak existence.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Three months after an interview, Derville, a notary, is approached by Crottat, who requests the return of 600 francs given to an old soldier, Colonel Chabert. Derville expresses his reluctance to continue supporting the soldier financially, having already been misled by his sense of patriotism. He notices a letter on his desk, written in German, and instructs his clerk Boucard to have it translated. The letter reveals that documents regarding Chabert's estate will soon arrive, confirming their legitimacy and the existence of witnesses in Prussia.

Derville recalls that the settlement of Chabert’s estate was handled by Roguin, a disreputable lawyer who recently absconded with clients' money. Crottat confirms that Chabert's widow, Rose Chapotel, inherited a personal fortune of about 300,000 francs after a contested will. Derville learns that Chabert is living in poor conditions in Faubourg Saint-Marceau with a dairyman named Vergniaud. 

Upon visiting Chabert, Derville finds him living in squalor, yet the Colonel maintains a sense of dignity. Chabert expresses his desire to reclaim his life and fortune, believing he deserves recognition as a general. Derville explains the complexities of proving Chabert's identity legally, including the potential for a lengthy and costly court battle. He warns Chabert that his wife, now married to Ferraud, may contest his claims, especially since they have children together.

Chabert is disheartened by the prospect of a drawn-out legal struggle and the financial burden it entails. Derville reassures him that they will pursue a friendly suit to annul both the death certificate and the marriage, aiming to restore Chabert's rights. Chabert agrees to trust Derville with his case.

As Derville leaves, he encounters Vergniaud, who expresses concern for Chabert's well-being and requests financial help to improve the Colonel's living conditions. Derville decides to provide assistance but insists that Chabert should be the one to help Vergniaud.

Derville then plans to confront Madame Ferraud, Chabert's widow, to gauge her reaction to the news of Chabert's survival. He reflects on the political and social dynamics surrounding the Ferrauds, particularly how Ferraud's ambitions and connections could influence the outcome of Chabert's case.

When Derville meets Madame Ferraud, she initially dismisses the idea of Chabert being alive. However, Derville's serious demeanor and knowledge of the situation quickly shift the tone of the conversation. He warns her of the legal implications of Chabert's return and the potential for public opinion to turn against her if the case goes to court.

Madame Ferraud, realizing the gravity of her situation, becomes defensive but is ultimately intrigued by the possibility of leveraging Chabert's affection to negotiate a favorable outcome. Derville proposes a compromise, suggesting that they could settle the matter amicably, which would benefit both parties.

The conversation reveals the underlying tensions in Madame Ferraud's life, including her fears of losing her status and wealth. Derville's strategic approach highlights the complexities of the legal system and the personal stakes involved for all parties. Ultimately, he leaves the meeting with a sense of confidence that he can navigate the situation to Chabert's advantage.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
A week after Derville's visits, Colonel Chabert and his wife, Madame la Comtesse Ferraud, set out from opposite ends of Paris to meet at Derville's office. Chabert, looking dignified in a new outfit and a wig, arrived in a cab, embodying the soldierly style he had regained. Meanwhile, the Countess, dressed elegantly, arrived shortly after. The office remained unchanged, with clerks joking about the peculiar situation.

Derville prepared a memorandum for the couple, outlining the terms of their agreement regarding Chabert's identity and the conditions for their marriage. The Countess was initially resistant to the idea of a lawsuit to annul Chabert's death certificate, insisting she wanted to avoid legal proceedings. When Chabert entered the room, the tension escalated, revealing the complexities of their relationship. The Countess feigned ignorance about Chabert's identity, but her reaction indicated otherwise.

As the discussion progressed, Chabert expressed his anger over the Countess's reluctance to acknowledge him and the financial terms she proposed. The Countess, feeling cornered, left abruptly, prompting Derville to caution Chabert about her potential traps. Despite his rage, Chabert was deeply affected by the encounter and left the office in despair.

Later, the Countess approached Chabert, leading him to her carriage. They traveled to her country house, where she expressed her emotional turmoil over their situation. The Countess revealed her love for another man, Comte Ferraud, complicating their reunion. Chabert, though hurt, offered his understanding and support, suggesting they maintain dignity and avoid public scandal.

During their time together, the Countess attempted to charm Chabert, seeking to erase the pain of their past. However, her ulterior motives became apparent as she plotted to secure her position while keeping Chabert at bay. After several days, Chabert began to sense her manipulations, leading to a confrontation with Delbecq, the Countess's secretary, who suggested exploiting Chabert's situation for financial gain.

Feeling betrayed, Chabert confronted the Countess, who attempted to manipulate him further. He ultimately decided to sacrifice his claim to his identity for her happiness, but the Countess's true intentions were revealed when she discussed putting him in an asylum if necessary. This realization shattered Chabert's trust, leading him to reject her and resolve to disappear from her life.

Chabert's subsequent life was marked by hardship, as he fell into poverty and obscurity. Derville, unaware of Chabert's fate, assumed the couple had reached a compromise. However, he later discovered Chabert imprisoned as a vagabond, stripped of his dignity. Despite his circumstances, Chabert maintained a sense of pride and contempt for societal norms.

In a final act of integrity, Chabert sought to repay Derville for his assistance, demonstrating his enduring loyalty despite the betrayal he faced. Derville, recognizing Chabert's plight, facilitated the payment owed to him by the Countess, highlighting the tragic consequences of their entangled lives. Ultimately, Chabert's story serves as a poignant reflection on identity, love, and the harsh realities of life after war.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
In June 1840, attorney Godeschal visited Bicetre with Derville, his predecessor. They encountered an elderly pauper, who was drying his handkerchief in the sun. Godeschal remarked on the man's resemblance to grotesque carvings, while Derville revealed that the man was Colonel Chabert, the estranged husband of the Comtesse Ferraud. Derville recounted how Chabert had been sent to the workhouse, a fate stemming from his reminder of their humble beginnings.

Two days later, they returned to Bicetre and found Chabert drawing in the sand. When addressed, he insisted he was no longer Chabert but "Hyacinthe," and expressed a childlike innocence. Godeschal offered him money for snuff, which Chabert gratefully accepted, displaying a mix of military bravado and confusion.

An observing pauper noted that Chabert, despite his apparent childishness, was clever and philosophical. He recounted a past encounter where Chabert had impressed a Prussian officer with his military history. Derville lamented the fate of men like Chabert, who had served Napoleon only to end up in a workhouse.

Derville reflected on the disillusionment faced by priests, doctors, and lawyers, particularly the latter, who witness the darkest aspects of human nature. He shared harrowing experiences from his legal career, including familial betrayals and moral corruption, concluding that the horrors he had seen surpassed any fictional accounts. Godeschal, having seen similar cases, expressed his desire to escape the grim realities of Paris for a quieter life in the countryside.

The narrative highlights the tragic decline of Colonel Chabert and critiques societal indifference towards those who have fallen from grace, emphasizing the darker truths of human relationships and the legal profession.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

**The Atheist's Mass**  
*By Honoré De Balzac*  
*Translated by Clara Bell*  

**Dedicated to Auguste Borget by his friend De Balzac**

**Summary:**

Bianchon, a prominent physician in Paris, initially trained as a surgeon under the renowned Desplein, whose genius in surgery was unmatched but ultimately transient. Desplein's brilliance, while celebrated, did not translate into lasting legacy; he lacked the ability to pass on his knowledge, and his atheism, shaped by his scientific observations, left him isolated in his beliefs. He viewed the world through a lens of skepticism, rejecting the notion of a soul, which he never found in his dissections.

Despite his genius, Desplein's life was marked by contradictions that his detractors seized upon. His eccentricities, such as fluctuating between flamboyant and indifferent dress, and his complex personality, led to mixed judgments about him. He was both generous and miserly, often displaying a deep contempt for humanity after scrutinizing their behaviors.

Among his students, Bianchon stood out, having endured poverty while studying medicine. He was a loyal friend, known for his integrity and willingness to help others without expecting anything in return. Desplein recognized Bianchon's potential and took him under his wing, introducing him to the intricacies of medical practice and the elite of Paris.

One day, Bianchon witnessed Desplein entering a church, which surprised him given Desplein's staunch atheism. This incident sparked Bianchon's curiosity, leading him to question Desplein's beliefs. During a dinner conversation, Desplein dismissed the significance of the mass, criticizing its historical implications and the bloodshed associated with its establishment.

Months later, another physician questioned Desplein about his church visit, to which Desplein provided a vague excuse. Bianchon, intrigued, decided to observe Desplein's behavior over time. The following year, he confirmed Desplein's attendance at mass again, discovering that Desplein had founded the very service he attended.

This revelation deepened the mystery surrounding Desplein, highlighting the complexity of his character—a man who, despite his atheistic convictions, engaged in rituals that contradicted his beliefs. Bianchon was left pondering the enigmatic nature of Desplein's actions, which seemed to defy the straightforwardness of his scientific reasoning.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Doctor Bianchon took seven years to discuss a significant incident from his friend Desplein's life. After the Revolution of 1830, amidst chaos in Paris, Bianchon spotted Desplein entering Saint-Sulpice and joined him for mass. Curious about Desplein's attendance, given his atheism, Bianchon pressed for an explanation.

Desplein revealed that he had endured extreme poverty during his early years, living in a dilapidated building where he faced hunger, isolation, and the struggle to finance his medical education. He described his life as a relentless battle against despair, with no support from family or friends. His meager meals consisted of stale bread soaked in milk, and he often went without proper clothing or companionship.

Despite his hardships, Desplein was determined to succeed. He immersed himself in his studies, driven by the desire to escape his dire circumstances. He recounted how he felt misunderstood by those who had never experienced true poverty, likening their ignorance to a princess who suggested the hungry should simply buy cake.

Desplein's bitterness stemmed from witnessing the selfishness and envy of others, particularly in the competitive environment of Paris. He expressed a deep mistrust of humanity, feeling that mediocrity constantly undermined those striving for greatness.

During this challenging period, Desplein faced eviction due to unpaid rent. His neighbor, Bourgeat, a humble water-carrier, offered to help. Bourgeat paid Desplein's debts and shared his aspirations of buying a horse and barrel for his work. In return, he supported Desplein's education, providing food and encouragement, treating him like a son.

Desplein described Bourgeat as a devoted figure, embodying the virtues of selflessness and care. Their bond deepened over time, with Bourgeat taking on domestic responsibilities and nurturing Desplein's ambitions. When Desplein became a house surgeon, he felt a profound loss when he had to leave Bourgeat behind, but he remained committed to repaying his kindness.

Bourgeat fell ill, and Desplein dedicated himself to his care, trying every possible means to save him. Despite his efforts, Bourgeat eventually passed away, leaving Desplein heartbroken. He honored Bourgeat's wishes by arranging masses for his soul, reflecting on the man's unwavering faith and love.

Desplein's relationship with Bourgeat profoundly impacted his life, leading him to reconcile his skepticism with a sense of gratitude. He expressed a desire for Bourgeat's faith to inspire him, acknowledging the depth of their connection.

In the end, Bianchon, who witnessed Desplein's final days, could not definitively label him an atheist, pondering whether Bourgeat's faith might have opened the gates of Heaven for his friend. The narrative concludes with a reflection on the enduring bond between the two men, highlighting themes of sacrifice, gratitude, and the complexities of belief.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

**The Commission in Lunacy**  
*By Honoré De Balzac*  
*Translated by Clara Bell*  

**Dedication**  
Dedicated to Monsieur le Contre-Amiral Bazoche, Governor of the Isle of Bourbon, by the grateful writer.  

---

In 1828, at around one o’clock in the morning, Dr. Horace Bianchon and Baron de Rastignac left a house on Rue du Faubourg Saint-Honoré. They decided to walk to the boulevard, as neither had a carriage. Their conversation turned to the Marquise d’Espard, whom Rastignac admired. Bianchon, however, cautioned him against pursuing her, comparing her to a “one-eyed horse” and suggesting that she was not as youthful as Rastignac believed. 

Rastignac argued that the Marquise’s social status and wealth made her an attractive prospect, especially since he was struggling financially after marrying off his sisters. Bianchon, a physician, expressed his disdain for women of fashion, asserting that they often lacked genuine character and were driven by selfishness. He warned Rastignac that the Marquise might be in debt despite her outward charm.

Bianchon recounted his experiences with high society, describing the superficiality and moral decay he observed. He believed that a woman of true virtue would not be fashionable, as the qualities that elevate a woman in society often stem from vices. Rastignac countered that a woman of fashion could provide significant advantages for an ambitious man, likening her to a tool for success.

Despite Bianchon’s warnings, Rastignac remained enamored with the idea of marrying the Marquise, valuing her social connections over the virtues of a more devoted partner. Bianchon reluctantly agreed to help Rastignac by introducing him to his uncle, Judge Jean-Jules Popinot, who was known for his strict adherence to the law.

The next morning, Bianchon set out to visit Popinot, who lived in a dilapidated area of Paris. The judge was characterized by his unkempt appearance and lack of ambition, having been overlooked for promotions despite his legal acumen. His life was marked by a commitment to justice, often at the expense of his own comfort and reputation.

Popinot was a diligent and compassionate judge, known for his ability to discern the truth in legal matters. He was respected for his knowledge of criminal law but remained a supernumerary judge due to his modest nature and lack of political maneuvering. His life was a testament to the struggles of a good man in a flawed system, where ambition often overshadowed merit.

As Bianchon approached Popinot’s residence, he reflected on the challenges of persuading the judge to assist Rastignac. Despite the difficulties, he was determined to fulfill his friend’s request, aware of the complexities involved in navigating the world of high society and the legal system. 

In summary, the narrative explores themes of ambition, social status, and the moral dilemmas faced by individuals in pursuit of success. Bianchon’s skepticism about the Marquise serves as a counterpoint to Rastignac’s aspirations, highlighting the tension between genuine character and societal expectations. The story ultimately critiques the superficiality of fashionable society while portraying the struggles of a principled man within the judicial system.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Twelve years prior to the events of this story, in 1816, Popinot was appointed President of the Commission Extraordinary to distribute food to the impoverished in his neighborhood during a severe scarcity. Despite his initial reluctance to remain in the Rue du Fouarre, he became deeply involved in alleviating the suffering around him. Over five years, he transformed from a judge focused solely on legal outcomes to a compassionate figure, akin to Saint Vincent de Paul, dedicated to helping the poor. His charitable work consumed his resources, but he found fulfillment in his role as a protector and benefactor to those in need.

By the end of his second year of service, Popinot had converted his storeroom into a parlor for the needy, where he meticulously recorded donations and requests for assistance. His aide, Lavienne, helped manage the influx of people seeking aid, which filled the room with the desperate and destitute from dawn until late morning. The atmosphere was often stifling, yet the poor revered Popinot, who worked tirelessly to provide them with food, shelter, and employment.

Popinot's modest inherited fortune and his late wife's wealth allowed him to live frugally while dedicating himself to charity. He was known for his absent-mindedness regarding personal appearance, a trait often associated with intellectual pursuits. Despite his significant contributions to society, he remained unrecognized by his peers, having not received the Legion of Honor.

The narrative shifts to a case involving the Marquis d’Espard, whose wife sought a Commission in Lunacy for him. Bianchon, a doctor and Popinot's nephew, visits the Rue du Fouarre, where he finds his uncle amidst a crowd of impoverished individuals. The scene is vivid, with various characters representing the struggles of the poor, each with their own stories of hardship.

Popinot's attire is disheveled, reflecting his preoccupation with his charitable work. He engages with the petitioners, providing assistance where he can, while Lavienne manages the flow of people. Bianchon expresses concern about the state of the Marquis d’Espard, and Popinot reads the petition detailing the Marquis's alleged mental decline and the influence of a woman named Jeanrenaud over him.

The petition outlines the Marquis's erratic behavior, financial mismanagement, and obsession with China, suggesting that he may be under some form of undue influence. Popinot and Bianchon discuss the implications of the petition, with Bianchon suggesting that the influence could be akin to a form of possession. Popinot remains skeptical but acknowledges the need to investigate further.

As the conversation unfolds, Popinot reflects on the nature of the allegations and the potential motivations behind them. He considers the possibility that the Marquise may have ulterior motives in seeking the Commission in Lunacy for her husband. Ultimately, Bianchon persuades Popinot to visit Madame d’Espard to hear her side of the story, emphasizing the importance of understanding both perspectives in the case.

Popinot agrees to the visit, recognizing the necessity of gathering all relevant information before making a judgment. The chapter concludes with a sense of anticipation for the upcoming meeting, highlighting the complexities of human relationships and the challenges of navigating the legal and moral implications of the case.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The following evening, Bianchon climbed his uncle Popinot's staircase to find him working on a complex judgment. Popinot, dressed in an old, stained coat due to a delay from the tailor, appeared disheveled, but Bianchon managed to straighten him up a bit before they entered the Marquise’s room.

Madame d’Espard had been a prominent figure in Paris for seven years, having separated from her husband in 1815. At thirty-three, she maintained a youthful appearance through strict beauty regimens, reminiscent of historical figures like Diane de Poitiers. Her efforts to preserve her looks were so extreme that they drew comparisons to a famous Polish lady known for her longevity and vitality. Despite being a mother of two, Madame d’Espard was still considered fashionable, largely due to her social connections and the absence of visible signs of aging.

After her separation, Madame d’Espard lived a quiet life until 1820, when she re-entered society, hosting gatherings and establishing herself among the elite. She became close with influential figures, including a duchess and a countess, which helped her gain political influence. Her drawing-room became a hub for ambitious men, and she skillfully navigated the complexities of Parisian social life, maintaining a façade of innocence while wielding considerable power.

Popinot and Bianchon arrived at the Marquise’s boudoir, where she sat elegantly, flanked by Rastignac and a silent man, the Chevalier d’Espard. The Marquise’s appearance was striking, with a carefully curated environment that reflected her status. Popinot, however, was focused on the case at hand, which involved her estranged husband, M. d’Espard, whom she claimed was mentally unstable.

As the conversation unfolded, Madame d’Espard recounted her version of events leading to their separation, painting herself as a victim of her husband’s whims. Popinot, however, probed deeper, questioning her motives and the financial implications of her situation. He noted the luxurious lifestyle she maintained, which seemed at odds with her claims of financial distress. The Marquise attempted to deflect his inquiries, but Popinot remained steadfast, suggesting that her lavish spending could indicate ulterior motives in her petition for her husband’s incapacity.

The tension in the room escalated as Popinot pressed for clarity on her financial situation, hinting that her request might be driven by self-interest rather than genuine concern for her children. The Marquise, feeling cornered, defended her actions as necessary for securing her children’s future, but Popinot’s scrutiny revealed cracks in her narrative.

After their meeting, Popinot resolved to investigate further, planning to summon Madame Jeanrenaud, the woman linked to M. d’Espard, to uncover more about the situation. The next day, Madame Jeanrenaud arrived, presenting herself as a comically unassuming figure, far from the seductive temptress implied by the Marquise. She expressed concern for M. d’Espard, indicating a willingness to cooperate, which left Popinot intrigued about the true dynamics at play.

As the story unfolded, it became clear that the Marquise’s polished exterior masked a complex web of ambition and manipulation, while Popinot’s role as a judge required him to navigate these treacherous waters with caution and discernment. The case promised to reveal not only the truth about M. d’Espard’s mental state but also the lengths to which individuals would go to protect their interests in the cutthroat world of Parisian society.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The text explores the life of M. le Marquis d’Espard, a nobleman living in a dilapidated mansion in Paris, and the impact of seemingly trivial events on significant outcomes. Following a minor illness, the judge's absence delayed a crucial meeting, paralleling historical events where small incidents led to major consequences.

D’Espard's home, once grand, had fallen into disrepair, reflecting the decline of nobility in France. The house, located in a steep area, was chosen for its garden, which benefited his children’s health. Despite its state, d’Espard managed to create a peaceful and dignified environment, with modest yet tasteful decor that conveyed a sense of order and tranquility.

His family life was private, with only a few loyal servants. D’Espard’s decision to isolate his children from the outside world led to misunderstandings among neighbors, who viewed his behavior as eccentric. Gossip painted him as a madman, exacerbated by his frugal lifestyle and the perception that he was financially ruined. This reputation was fueled by the tenants’ jealousy and resentment towards his noble status, which they believed was undeserved without wealth.

D’Espard and his wife were true aristocrats, embodying the values of their lineage. They instilled a sense of dignity and pride in their children, who exhibited the characteristics of their noble heritage. The Marquis, at around fifty, presented a refined appearance but was perceived as cold and eccentric due to his mannerisms and speech. His children, Comte Clement and Vicomte Camille, were handsome and graceful, reflecting their noble upbringing. Clement, nearing sixteen, was transitioning into young adulthood, while Camille was still in a more youthful stage of education.

Overall, the narrative illustrates the complexities of social status, the impact of isolation, and the misunderstandings that arise from societal perceptions, particularly regarding the declining nobility in a changing France.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
On a Thursday holiday, Popinot planned to question M. d’Espard. Early in the morning, his sons, Clement and Camille, played in the garden, wrestling and making noise, which woke their father, the Marquis. The Marquis enjoyed watching them, finding joy in their playful antics. However, the portress, upon Popinot's arrival, informed him of the Marquis' supposed madness, claiming he encouraged his children to fight.

Popinot was led to the third floor, where he encountered a shabby office filled with papers and an old clerk. After a brief introduction, Popinot met the Marquis, who was reading by the fire. The judge explained that he was there to investigate a petition for a Commission in Lunacy filed by the Marquise d’Espard. The Marquis, initially taken aback, regained his composure and asked why he had not been notified of the petition. Popinot explained that such notifications were unnecessary for those deemed insane.

The Marquis expressed concern about the implications of the inquiry on his wife. Popinot assured him of confidentiality and asked for an explanation regarding allegations of financial impropriety involving a Madame Jeanrenaud. The Marquis, visibly distressed, revealed a family secret tied to the Jeanrenaud family, whose fortune had been wrongfully acquired during the Revocation of the Edict of Nantes. He explained that his ancestors had benefited from the execution of a Protestant merchant, and he felt compelled to make restitution to the Jeanrenauds, whom he had located in France.

Despite his wife's lack of understanding, the Marquis had committed to repaying the Jeanrenauds, which strained his finances. He had chosen to educate his sons himself, sacrificing his social standing to ensure they grew up with integrity. The judge listened intently, moved by the Marquis' noble intentions and the burden of his family's past.

As the conversation continued, the Marquis invited Popinot to his home, revealing that he had rented the office space for his publishing venture on China, which was financially successful. He expressed a desire to provide for his children while maintaining their honor. Just then, his sons returned from riding, full of energy and affection for their father, which further illustrated the strong bond they shared.

Madame Jeanrenaud soon entered, expressing her desire to return the money given to the Marquis, fearing for their honor. Popinot intervened, explaining that the payments were legally justified and rooted in honorable motives. He reassured the Marquis that his actions would not be misinterpreted and praised him for his integrity.

Afterward, Popinot returned to his office, reflecting on the encounter and the nobility of the Marquis. The next day, he was summoned by the President of his Court, who informed him that he would be removed from the case due to a potential conflict of interest stemming from his visit with Madame d’Espard. Although Popinot protested, he ultimately accepted the decision, recognizing the importance of maintaining the Court's integrity.

In summary, the narrative explores themes of honor, familial duty, and the complexities of social standing, as the Marquis d’Espard grapples with his family's dark history while striving to restore their honor through acts of restitution and dedication to his children.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

**Pierre Grassou**  
*By Honoré De Balzac*  
*Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley*

**Dedication**  
To The Lieutenant-Colonel of Artillery, Periollas, As a Testimony of the Affectionate Esteem of the Author, De Balzac

---

In the wake of the 1830 revolution, the art exhibition landscape has drastically changed, leading to a sense of unease among viewers. The once prestigious Salon has devolved into a chaotic bazaar, losing its ability to showcase the finest works of art. The public, overwhelmed by the sheer volume of submissions, finds it difficult to engage with the art, leading to a lack of passionate discourse that once surrounded celebrated artists like Delacroix and Ingres. The Salon's expansion has diluted its quality, resulting in a muddled display where great artists struggle to stand out.

The current state of the art world is marked by complaints of unrecognized genius, despite the fact that many mediocre works are now accepted. The absence of a discerning jury has led to a decline in artistic standards, and without a proper selection process, the future of art is at risk.

Among the lesser-known artists is Pierre Grassou, a painter from Fougeres, who embodies the struggles of many in the art community. In 1832, Grassou lived in a modest studio in Paris, characterized by its tidy yet simple furnishings. He was a genre painter, content with his limited ambitions and lacking the desire to pursue high art.

One December morning, while preparing to paint, Grassou was visited by Elie Magus, a picture dealer known for his shrewdness. Magus proposed that Grassou paint portraits of a bourgeois family—the Vervelles—who were eager to enter the art world. The family included a daughter with a substantial dowry, which piqued Grassou's interest, despite his initial reluctance to marry.

Grassou's background reveals a diligent but unremarkable artist. He studied under various masters, including Servin and Schinner, but never distinguished himself. His gentle demeanor and mediocrity earned him the nickname "Fougeres," which he accepted with humility. After moving to Paris, he struggled to establish himself, facing rejection at his first exhibition in 1819 with a painting that failed to impress the jury.

Instead of succumbing to despair, Grassou sought feedback from Schinner, who candidly advised him to abandon painting due to his lack of talent. Undeterred, Grassou revised his work but ultimately sold it to Magus for a meager sum. This transaction marked the beginning of his career, albeit one filled with challenges and modest success.

Grassou's life reflects the broader struggles of artists in a changing cultural landscape, where the value of art is increasingly questioned. His journey illustrates the tension between ambition and reality, as he navigates the complexities of the art world while remaining true to his humble origins.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For several days, Pierre Fougeres lingered near Elie's shop, hoping for recognition of his painting, which ultimately disappeared. When he inquired, Elie informed him it had sold, offering to buy more of his work. Overjoyed, Fougeres felt validated in his talent, despite the skepticism of others. He returned to his studio, inspired, and worked tirelessly on new pieces, eventually producing four paintings that were critiqued as imitative.

Despite the criticism, Elie bought the paintings for a modest price, allowing Fougeres to live frugally while continuing to create art. After years of hard work, he gained some recognition at the 1829 Exhibition, where one of his paintings, depicting a condemned man, garnered significant attention and was purchased by Madame la Dauphine. This success marked a turning point in his career, leading to further commissions and a modest income.

Fougeres, now financially stable, furnished a studio and continued to produce art, though he remained humble and dedicated to his craft. His friends admired his perseverance, even if they found his work lacking in originality. He maintained a simple lifestyle, investing his earnings with a notary, Cardot, and dreaming of greater artistic achievements and recognition.

One day, the Vervelle family, wealthy and eager for portraits, approached Fougeres. Their admiration for him flattered the artist, who found himself drawn to their daughter, Virginie. As he painted their portraits, he became increasingly enchanted by her, despite his initial reservations about their social status. The Vervelles, impressed by his reputation and financial stability, began to consider him as a potential match for their daughter.

As the sittings progressed, the Vervelles grew more familiar with Fougeres, who reciprocated their enthusiasm. They discussed their art collection and praised his work, further solidifying their interest in him. The notary's positive remarks about Fougeres' character and financial prudence added to the family's favorable view, making them more inclined to see him as a suitable partner for Virginie.

Fougeres, caught up in the charm of the Vervelle family and his growing feelings for Virginie, began to envision a future with her. He rationalized that wealth and stability were essential for happiness, leading him to believe that marrying into the Vervelle family could fulfill his dreams. As he painted, he contemplated the possibilities of love and success intertwined with his artistic ambitions.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
On the day Fougeres began painting Mademoiselle Virginie, he was effectively part of the Vervelle family. The Vervelles felt a strong connection to the studio, which they considered a second home. During a sitting, Joseph Bridau burst in, disheveled and frantic, seeking financial help from Grassou, who was painting Virginie. Bridau's chaotic entrance shocked the Vervelles, who turned red with embarrassment. He requested 500 francs, claiming creditors were after him, and Grassou reluctantly agreed to write a note for his notary.

As Bridau critiqued Grassou's work, he encouraged him to embrace nature in his painting. After Bridau left, Madame Vervelle expressed concern about the impact of his visit on Virginie's portrait, but Grassou reassured her that it had only improved. Vervelle, eager to impress the artist, invited Fougeres to their country house, hoping to provide an enjoyable experience.

Grassou accepted the invitation and was welcomed warmly by the Vervelles at their villa in Ville d'Avray. They proudly showcased their meticulously maintained grounds and prepared a lavish dinner. Afterward, they led Grassou to their picture gallery, where he was astonished to find many of his own paintings misattributed to famous masters like Rubens and Rembrandt. 

As Grassou feigned admiration, he realized the extent of the deception. Vervelle boasted about the value of his collection, and Grassou privately revealed that he had sold those paintings to the dealer Elie Magus for a fraction of their supposed worth. Vervelle, impressed, promised to double his daughter's dowry if Grassou could prove his claims.

Despite the absurdity of the situation, Grassou's reputation grew. He became known as a fine portrait painter, earning a comfortable living and marrying into the Vervelle family. He lived happily with them, maintaining a successful career while grappling with the insecurity of being mocked by fellow artists. Grassou continued to aspire to join the Academy, all the while secretly replacing his own lesser works in the Vervelle collection with those of more renowned artists.

Grassou's life exemplified the complexities of mediocrity and ambition in the art world, where he was both respected and ridiculed. He remained a devoted husband and father, yet he could not shake the feeling that his artistic peers looked down on him. Nevertheless, he persisted in his work, hoping to achieve greater recognition and success.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################

################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################

**EUGENIE GRANDET**  
By Honore De Balzac  
Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley  

**DEDICATION**  
To Maria. May your name, that of one whose portrait is the noblest ornament of this work, lie on its opening pages like a branch of sacred box, taken from an unknown tree, but sanctified by religion, and kept ever fresh and green by pious hands to bless the house.  
—De Balzac.

---

**EUGENIE GRANDET**

**I**

In certain provincial towns, some houses evoke a profound melancholy, reminiscent of cloisters or ruins. One such house in Saumur, at the end of a steep street leading to the chateau, embodies this sadness. The street, now seldom traveled, is characterized by its narrowness and the silence of its ancient homes, which are over three centuries old. These houses, with their carved oaken beams and worn window-sills, tell the history of France through their architecture.

Next to a dilapidated artisan's home stands the mansion of Monsieur Grandet, a country gentleman whose door bears the remnants of armorial bearings. The ground-floors of merchants in this area are not shops but dark, deep rooms with no glass, where goods are displayed in a simple manner. The inhabitants of Saumur, deeply connected to the land, are acutely aware of the weather's impact on their livelihoods, with conversations revolving around the state of the harvest and the market.

On Saturdays, merchants leave for their vineyards, leaving the streets empty. Life unfolds outdoors, with neighbors observing each other closely. The old town of Saumur, once home to the local nobility, houses the Grandet family, whose story is central to this narrative.

Monsieur Grandet, known as le Pere Grandet, was a master cooper who became wealthy during the Revolution by purchasing church properties. With his wife’s dowry, he acquired prime vineyards and farms, becoming a respected figure in Saumur. His wealth grew through shrewd investments and a keen understanding of the wine trade, making him a significant player in the local economy.

By 1816, Grandet's estate was estimated at nearly four million francs, with many believing he had hidden treasures. His reputation was built on his financial acumen and his ability to manipulate the market, earning him both admiration and fear. He lived frugally, relying on his land for sustenance and employing only a few trusted individuals.

Grandet's demeanor was simple and unassuming, yet he commanded respect. He rarely spoke, preferring to communicate through short, decisive phrases. His stutter, often mistaken for a lack of education, was a calculated facade. He was a man of routine, dressed consistently in practical clothing, and his life revolved around his daughter, Eugenie, whom he intended to marry off advantageously.

The social dynamics of Saumur were influenced by the competition for Eugenie's hand in marriage. Two families, the Cruchots and the des Grassins, vied for her favor, each believing they could secure a more advantageous match. The Cruchots, led by the ambitious C. de Bonfons, sought to align with the Grandets through marriage, while the des Grassins, represented by the charming Adolphe, aimed to win Eugenie’s heart.

As the story unfolds, the tension between these families escalates, with the stakes raised by the potential inheritance of Eugenie’s fortune. The arrival of the estate of Froidfond for sale creates further intrigue, as both factions see it as a means to elevate their status and secure Eugenie’s future.

Monsieur Grandet, seizing the opportunity, purchases the estate, further solidifying his wealth and influence. This acquisition sends ripples through Saumur, as the townspeople speculate on the implications for the Grandet family and the ongoing competition for Eugenie’s hand.

In this world of ambition, avarice, and social maneuvering, the fate of Eugenie Grandet hangs in the balance, reflecting the complexities of love, wealth, and familial duty in a changing society.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**Summary:**

The house of Monsieur Grandet is a cold, silent structure made of tufa stone, resembling a jail with its weathered archway and crumbling bas-relief of the four seasons. The entrance features a solid oak door with a rusty grating, leading to a dark vault and a damp garden enclosed by thick walls. The main room, a large hall, serves multiple purposes—an antechamber, salon, office, and dining room—where local townsfolk conduct business with Grandet. The hall is adorned with gray wooden panels, an old brass clock, and faded tapestries, reflecting the family's modest lifestyle.

Madame Grandet and their daughter, Eugenie, live under strict conditions, with Grandet controlling their resources tightly. They manage household tasks diligently, often working late into the night. La Grande Nanon, their servant, has been with the family for thirty-five years, earning a meager wage but saving enough to secure her future. Despite her rough appearance, she is devoted to Grandet, who treats her with a mix of disdain and grudging affection.

As the story unfolds, it is November 1819, and the family prepares to celebrate Eugenie's birthday. Grandet presents her with a gold coin, while Madame Grandet gives her a new dress. The family dinner is interrupted by the arrival of local notables, including the Cruchots and the des Grassins, who bring gifts for Eugenie. The des Grassins' son, Adolphe, offers her a workbox, which delights her, while Grandet remains focused on his wealth and the potential marriage prospects for Eugenie.

The evening progresses with card games, revealing the underlying tensions and ambitions of the guests, all vying for Grandet's favor and wealth. The atmosphere is a mix of familial warmth and the cold calculations of social status and financial gain. Grandet's miserly nature dominates the scene, overshadowing genuine emotions, as the women in the household remain blissfully unaware of the true extent of his wealth and the machinations surrounding them.

As the night continues, the sound of a loud knock at the door interrupts the festivities, signaling an unexpected visitor and heightening the tension in the household.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Nanon lit a candle and opened the door, followed by her master, Monsieur Grandet. His wife, alarmed, called out to him, while the players exchanged worried glances. Monsieur des Grassins suggested they all go, sensing something ominous. When a young man arrived with a porter carrying trunks, Grandet brusquely told his wife to return to the game, leaving the players uneasy.

The newcomer was Charles Grandet, the son of Grandet from Paris. He was handsome and dressed in the latest fashion, which contrasted sharply with the provincial attire of the others. As he entered, he greeted the company with grace, but Grandet's stern demeanor overshadowed the warm welcome. Madame Grandet offered him hospitality, but Grandet interrupted, insisting he needed no refreshment.

Charles, having been sent to Saumur for the first time, aimed to impress with his Parisian sophistication. He brought an extravagant collection of clothing and accessories, intending to showcase his status. However, he was unaware of the modesty of his uncle's home and the provincial lifestyle that awaited him.

The provincial guests, including the Cruchots and des Grassins, were taken aback by Charles's elegance. They scrutinized him with a mix of admiration and disdain, feeling both intrigued and threatened by his presence. Eugenie Grandet, Charles's cousin, was particularly captivated by him, feeling a longing for the refinement he represented, contrasting sharply with her own mundane life.

As the game of loto concluded, Nanon returned to announce preparations for Charles's room. Eugenie, eager to impress her cousin, rushed to help with the arrangements, suggesting improvements to make his stay more comfortable. She even proposed buying a wax candle, a luxury her father would typically deny.

Meanwhile, Madame des Grassins attempted to charm Charles, hinting at social gatherings that could alleviate his boredom in Saumur. She subtly criticized Grandet's miserly nature and suggested that Charles would find more enjoyment outside his uncle's home. The conversation hinted at potential romantic interests, with Madame des Grassins positioning herself as a matchmaker.

As the evening progressed, Grandet received a letter revealing a family tragedy: his brother was bankrupt and dying, leaving his son Charles in a precarious situation. The letter implored Grandet to care for Charles, emphasizing the urgency of the request. Grandet's reaction was one of mixed emotions, as he concealed his thoughts while contemplating the implications of his brother's demise.

After the guests departed, Grandet's demeanor shifted, revealing a man grappling with familial duty and the weight of financial responsibility. The evening had set the stage for a complex interplay of relationships, ambitions, and the looming specter of wealth and poverty that would shape the lives of all involved. 

In summary, the arrival of Charles Grandet in Saumur disrupts the provincial status quo, igniting curiosity and ambition among the local elite, while also unveiling the burdens of family legacy and the expectations of wealth.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
**Summary of the Text**

Monsieur Grandet, a miserly man, instructs his nephew Charles about the household routine upon his arrival. He emphasizes his hard work and lack of wealth, despite rumors of his riches. Charles, feeling out of place in his uncle's shabby home, is shown to his room by Grandet, who warns him about the ferocious dog. Charles is disheartened by the dilapidated state of the house and questions why his father sent him there.

As Charles settles in, he reflects on his situation, wondering about the purpose of his visit. Meanwhile, Eugenie, Grandet's daughter, experiences a newfound awareness of her feelings as she prepares for the day. She admires her appearance and feels a sense of hope and excitement, particularly regarding her cousin Charles. However, she also struggles with self-doubt, believing she is not beautiful enough for him.

Eugenie's morning is filled with thoughts of Charles, and she seeks to impress him by asking Nanon, the servant, to prepare a special breakfast. Grandet, however, is stingy with resources and engages in a comical argument with Nanon over provisions. Despite his frugality, he begrudgingly allows Nanon to make a galette for the young ones.

As the day progresses, Grandet takes Eugenie for a walk, during which they encounter Cruchot, the notary. Their conversation reveals Grandet's disdain for the idea of marrying Eugenie to Charles, stating he would rather throw her into the Loire than allow such a union. This declaration devastates Eugenie, who had begun to develop feelings for Charles.

Later, Grandet receives news of his brother's suicide, which shocks the family. Eugenie is deeply affected by the news, feeling compassion for Charles, who remains unaware of his father's fate. Grandet insists that Eugenie should not inform Charles until he returns, revealing his cold-heartedness.

Eugenie, overwhelmed by her emotions, confides in her mother, who understands her daughter's feelings but urges her to respect her father's wishes. Despite the tension, Eugenie expresses her affection for Charles and desires to prepare a special breakfast for him, showcasing her growing attachment.

As the narrative unfolds, the contrast between Grandet's miserly nature and the warmth of Eugenie's budding love for Charles becomes evident. The story captures the complexities of familial relationships, societal expectations, and the innocence of first love against a backdrop of financial hardship and emotional turmoil.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
After two hours of anxious preparation, Eugenie managed to create an unusual breakfast for her cousin Charles, which deviated from their family's customary standing meal of bread, fruit, and wine. She set the table with care, fearing her father's reaction if he returned to find such a spread. Madame Grandet reassured her, promising to take the blame if necessary. When Charles finally arrived, he was dressed flamboyantly and greeted them cheerfully, despite the recent destruction of his family estate.

As they sat down to eat, Charles expressed his hunger and desire for more extravagant food, which made Eugenie wish she could provide him with a partridge. Their conversation revealed the stark contrast between Charles's Parisian lifestyle and Eugenie's simple country life. Charles complimented Eugenie, making her blush, and they shared a moment of connection. However, the atmosphere shifted when Nanon brought in coffee, prompting Charles to comment on their outdated brewing method.

The mood darkened as they heard the familiar knock of Monsieur Grandet, Eugenie's father. The women panicked, quickly hiding evidence of the breakfast. Grandet entered, scrutinizing the scene and immediately sensing something was amiss. He chastised them for the lavish meal, unaware of its significance to Charles. As he prepared his own meager breakfast, he noticed the sugar and questioned its source, leading to a tense moment of silence among the women.

Charles, oblivious to the underlying tension, asked about his father's health, only to be met with the devastating news of his father's death by suicide. The revelation shattered him, and he wept openly, prompting sympathy from Eugenie and her mother. Grandet, however, remained detached, focusing on the financial implications of Charles's loss rather than the emotional devastation.

Eugenie, moved by Charles's grief, felt a growing affection for him. She and her mother decided to comfort him, but Charles was consumed by sorrow, lamenting his father's struggles. Eugenie encouraged him to think of his honor, trying to redirect his focus amidst his despair. The two women returned to their sewing, reflecting on the gravity of the situation and the stark contrast between their quiet lives and the turmoil surrounding them.

As they sewed in silence, Eugenie contemplated wearing mourning for her uncle, indicating her deepening connection to Charles and the shared grief that now bound them together.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
At around four o'clock, a sharp knock at the door startled Madame Grandet. She worried about her husband, but Grandet entered joyfully, revealing that he had sold his wine to a Belgian for a good price. He gleefully shared that he had made a significant profit, but when his daughter Eugenie suggested helping her cousin Charles, who was grieving for his father, Grandet's mood soured. He scolded Eugenie for even thinking of spending money on Charles, threatening to send her away if she mentioned it again.

Grandet, consumed by thoughts of investments and profits, retreated to his secret den, calculating his wealth, which had grown significantly. Meanwhile, Charles was mourning in his room, refusing to eat. Grandet dismissed the idea of mourning attire, insisting that true mourning was internal, not external. The family dinner was tense and silent, with Madame Grandet suggesting they should wear mourning, but Grandet scoffed at the expense.

Eugenie, feeling a surge of compassion for Charles, secretly visited him, offering comfort. She admired his handsome face, now marked by grief, and felt a deep connection to him. This encounter stirred feelings within her, awakening a desire for love and connection that she had never experienced before. 

The next morning, Grandet's demeanor shifted; he seemed more thoughtful and less harsh. He planned to manipulate the situation to his advantage, intending to help Charles while maintaining his own financial interests. He devised a scheme to impress the townspeople without spending his own money, using his brother's failure as a means to gain respect.

Eugenie busied herself with caring for Charles, bringing him breakfast and attending to his needs. Their bond deepened as they shared moments of tenderness amidst their respective sorrows. Charles, feeling grateful for their kindness, began to see Eugenie in a new light, appreciating her innocence and compassion.

As the day progressed, Grandet prepared to host a dinner for the Cruchots, signaling a rare moment of generosity. The household buzzed with activity as they set the table, and Charles, though still grieving, joined them, his presence marked by a quiet dignity. The atmosphere was charged with unspoken emotions, as Eugenie and Charles exchanged glances filled with understanding and hope, bridging the gap between their worlds through shared suffering and budding affection.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
At this moment, the town of Saumur was more intrigued by Grandet's dinner for the Cruchots than by the previous night's controversial wine sale. Grandet, a shrewd miser, paid little attention to public opinion, believing himself superior to the community he often deceived.

The des Grassins family soon learned of Guillaume Grandet's death and decided to visit Grandet that evening to express their condolences and to understand his motives for inviting the Cruchots. At dinner, the atmosphere was somber, with Grandet grave, Charles silent, and Eugenie and Madame Grandet saying little. After dinner, Charles excused himself to write letters, allowing Grandet to speak privately with the Cruchots.

Grandet, known for his cunning, employed his stutter and feigned deafness to manipulate the conversation, avoiding responsibility for his actions. He engaged in a discussion about bankruptcy with Monsieur C. de Bonfons, who attempted to guide him through the complexities of insolvency and liquidation. Grandet, while pretending to be confused, was actually assessing how to navigate his brother's financial troubles without tarnishing his own reputation.

The Cruchots suggested that Grandet could liquidate his brother's affairs to preserve his family's honor. Grandet, however, expressed his reluctance to engage in matters outside his expertise, insisting he was a simple wine-grower focused on his vines. The conversation turned to the possibility of buying up his brother's debts at a reduced rate, which Grandet found intriguing but complicated.

As the discussion progressed, the notary, concerned about the legality of the proposed actions, interrupted to caution against compromising their integrity. Just then, the des Grassins arrived, expressing their condolences for the Grandet family's misfortunes. The notary seized the opportunity to assert that Grandet intended to liquidate his brother's debts honorably, surprising the des Grassins, who had expected Grandet to act selfishly.

The des Grassins, impressed by Grandet's supposed nobility, offered to help manage the financial situation, but Grandet preferred to work with them rather than the Cruchots. He confided in the banker about his interest in investing in government bonds, indicating his desire to profit from the situation while maintaining a façade of familial duty.

After the des Grassins left, Grandet instructed his servant Nanon to prepare for a secretive journey. He planned to sell his gold in Angers, taking advantage of rising prices due to military preparations. Grandet's meticulousness ensured that no one in the neighborhood suspected his true intentions as he departed under the cover of night.

In summary, Grandet's dinner served as a backdrop for his cunning manipulation of those around him, as he navigated familial obligations and financial opportunities while maintaining his reputation as a respectable member of the community. His actions reflected a complex interplay of greed, deception, and the desire to uphold a facade of honor amidst personal gain.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Eugenie stood at the top of the stairs, reflecting on her father’s departure. The house fell silent, and she soon heard a cry from her cousin Charles's room. Concerned, she rushed upstairs, finding him asleep in an armchair, exhausted from writing letters. She noticed the addresses of various businesses, realizing he was preparing to leave France. Her heart sank as she spotted a letter addressed to "My dear Annette," igniting feelings of jealousy and despair.

Despite her initial resolve not to read it, curiosity overwhelmed her. As she read, she learned of Charles's tragic situation: his father had committed suicide, leaving him destitute. He expressed his determination to seek fortune in the Indies, lamenting that he could not return to Paris, where he would face scorn as the son of a bankrupt. Eugenie felt a surge of compassion for him, recognizing his plight and vowing to help.

She continued reading, discovering that Charles had no money for his journey or even a proper outfit. He contemplated sailing as a common sailor if necessary. The letter revealed his deep love for Annette but also his decision to part ways for her sake, believing it would be better for both of them. This revelation filled Eugenie with joy, as she interpreted it as a sign of his love for her.

Eugenie then found another letter addressed to Alphonse, where Charles detailed his financial ruin and requested assistance in settling his affairs. Moved by his situation, Eugenie decided to offer him her savings, which she had carefully accumulated. She retrieved a purse filled with gold coins, worth nearly six thousand francs, and approached Charles, who was initially hesitant to accept her gift.

Eugenie insisted, kneeling before him and imploring him to take the money. Overcome with emotion, Charles eventually agreed, recognizing the depth of their bond. He then revealed a precious family heirloom, a dressing case containing portraits of his parents, asking her to keep it safe. Their exchange solidified their connection, transcending mere familial ties.

As they shared this intimate moment, both felt a shift in their relationship. Charles, despite his misfortunes, began to see hope in their bond, while Eugenie vowed to support him. The next day, they spent time together, their mutual affection growing stronger amidst the backdrop of their respective family struggles.

Eugenie's father, Grandet, returned home with news of his financial dealings, having profited from the sale of gold. His jovial demeanor contrasted sharply with Charles's somber reality, highlighting the disparities in their lives. Grandet's focus on wealth and status overshadowed the emotional turmoil surrounding his daughter and nephew.

As the day unfolded, the tension between the characters became palpable. Charles grappled with his newfound responsibilities and the weight of his family's legacy, while Eugenie remained steadfast in her support. Their relationship blossomed against the backdrop of societal expectations and familial obligations, setting the stage for future challenges.

In the end, both Eugenie and Charles found solace in each other, their bond deepening as they navigated the complexities of love, loss, and the harsh realities of their world.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The following day, the Grandet family gathered for breakfast, creating a scene of domestic intimacy. Grief had united Madame Grandet, Eugenie, and Charles, while the old man, focused on his avarice, remained indifferent to their bond. He busied himself with his land and vineyards, leaving the young couple free to express their feelings under the watchful eye of Madame Grandet.

Eugenie experienced the awakening of love, having shared a secret with Charles that deepened their connection. Their relationship blossomed amidst the sadness of mourning, as they found joy in each other's company, exchanging tender glances and words. Charles, having previously known only the tumult of love, now embraced a pure affection for Eugenie and even the old house, which he had once found ridiculous.

After breakfast, while Grandet worked, Charles enjoyed the simple pleasure of being with Eugenie and her mother, appreciating their unworldly beauty. Eugenie became his ideal, reminiscent of Goethe's Margaret before her fall. Their love, innocent and childlike, was tinged with the awareness of impending separation.

Three days after des Grassins' departure, Grandet took Charles to the Civil courts to renounce his rights to his father's estate, a painful act for Charles. He also arranged for a passport and sold his belongings, which pleased Grandet. Charles, now dressed in mourning clothes, appeared more mature, and the family attended a Requiem for Guillaume Grandet.

During breakfast, Charles received letters from Paris, prompting Eugenie to inquire about his affairs. Grandet, irritated by the question, insisted on privacy. Later, in the garden, Charles revealed that his affairs were well-managed, and he was preparing to leave for the Indies. He expressed concern for Eugenie's future, suggesting she might find happiness elsewhere.

Eugenie, however, declared her love for him, and they shared a passionate kiss, interrupted by Nanon. As Charles prepared to leave, Grandet showed unexpected generosity, taking an interest in his nephew's departure and arranging for his belongings to be sent to Nantes.

The day of Charles's departure was filled with sadness. Nanon, despite her usual cheer, shed tears for the young man. The family escorted Charles to the diligence, where Grandet offered parting words of encouragement. As the coach departed, Eugenie wished for divine power to keep Charles close.

Meanwhile, Grandet engaged in financial dealings in Paris, acquiring a significant fortune through investments. His cunning allowed him to manage his brother's debts while maintaining his own reputation. Over time, creditors grew impatient, but Grandet skillfully delayed payments, leveraging their changing moods to his advantage.

As months passed, many creditors forgot their claims, and Grandet continued to manipulate the situation, ultimately selling his investments for a substantial profit. Des Grassins, now living in Paris, faced personal troubles, while Grandet distanced himself from any obligation to him.

In the aftermath of Charles's departure, Eugenie faced profound loneliness. She kept Charles's room as it was, hoping for his return. Her beauty transformed, reflecting the depth of her love. She followed his journey on a world map and mourned his absence, while maintaining a facade of cheerfulness around others.

Eugenie's love became a constant presence in her life, overshadowing her daily activities. Nanon, sympathetic to her mistress's plight, expressed her own frustrations about love, highlighting the sacrifices women make. Despite the pain, Eugenie's devotion to Charles remained unwavering, as she navigated the complexities of her emotions in a world that felt increasingly empty without him.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Two months passed, and the once monotonous domestic life of Eugenie and her mother, Madame Grandet, was now filled with the anxiety of a shared secret involving Charles, Eugenie's cousin. Eugenie often gazed at her aunt's portrait, searching for traces of Charles in her features. One Sunday, her mother discovered the truth about Eugenie's secret exchange of gold for Charles's affection, leading to a moment of panic about how they would face Grandet on New Year’s Day when he would expect to see Eugenie's gold.

As the new year approached, the women were consumed by dread. Madame Grandet, feeling the weight of her husband's anger and their precarious situation, suggested they avoid confronting him directly. On January 1, 1820, the cold winter morning prompted Madame Grandet to request a fire in her room, hoping to delay their visit to Grandet. Surprisingly, he agreed, displaying an unusual good humor that left both women bewildered.

Grandet's joy stemmed from a successful financial speculation, having received a substantial sum from Monsieur des Grassins. He reveled in his newfound wealth, oblivious to the turmoil brewing within his family. As he prepared for breakfast, he expressed his desire to see Eugenie's gold, which heightened the tension in the room. Eugenie, feeling the weight of her secret, suggested they eat first, hoping to postpone the inevitable confrontation.

After breakfast, Grandet pressed Eugenie for her gold, insisting she return it to him. When she claimed she no longer had it, he erupted in anger, accusing her of deceit and theft. Eugenie, desperate to protect her mother from Grandet's wrath, fell at her mother's feet, pleading for mercy. Madame Grandet, weakened by the confrontation, was taken to bed, leaving Eugenie to face her father alone.

Grandet's fury escalated as he demanded to know what Eugenie had done with her gold. She maintained her silence, asserting her independence as an adult. Grandet, unable to comprehend her defiance, threatened to confine her to her room until she submitted to his authority. Eugenie, heartbroken, retreated to her mother, who tried to comfort her despite her own suffering.

The confrontation between father and daughter reached a boiling point, with Grandet cursing Eugenie and accusing her of giving her fortune to Charles. He refused to forgive her, insisting she confess where her gold had gone. Madame Grandet, caught between her husband and daughter, implored him to show compassion, but Grandet remained resolute in his anger.

As the evening wore on, Grandet dined alone for the first time in years, while Nanon, the servant, tried to bring some comfort to Eugenie. The tension in the household was palpable, with Madame Grandet's health deteriorating under the strain of the conflict. The arrival of guests for New Year’s festivities only highlighted the absence of the women, raising suspicions about the family's troubles.

In the quiet of the night, Nanon brought Eugenie a special treat, a hare pâté, determined that she would not suffer on dry bread alone. This small act of kindness amidst the turmoil provided a glimmer of hope for Eugenie, who felt the weight of her choices and the love she held for Charles. The family dynamics, marked by love, fear, and the struggle for power, set the stage for a tragic unfolding of events in their lives.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
For several months, the old wine-grower, Grandet, visited his wife’s room without mentioning their daughter, Eugenie, who was confined to her room under harsh conditions. Madame Grandet's health deteriorated, and the townspeople speculated about the family's situation, eventually discovering that Eugenie was being kept on bread and water by her father's orders. Despite the town's condemnation of Grandet, Eugenie remained oblivious to the gossip, focusing instead on her love for Charles and her ailing mother.

Madame Grandet, aware of her impending death, expressed her desire for reconciliation between father and daughter. She urged Grandet to bring Eugenie back into the family fold, but he remained indifferent. As her illness progressed, Madame Grandet confided in the Cruchots about her daughter's treatment, prompting them to consider legal action against Grandet. However, Eugenie insisted on loyalty to her father, asking them to remain silent to protect the family honor.

As spring approached, Madame Grandet's condition worsened, and she sought help from the notary, Cruchot, who warned Grandet that if his wife died, Eugenie would inherit her mother’s estate, potentially forcing him to sell his property. This revelation shocked Grandet, who had never considered the legal implications of his daughter's inheritance. Cruchot advised him to treat Eugenie kindly to avoid conflict over the estate.

Grandet, realizing the gravity of the situation, decided to reconcile with Eugenie. He returned home with a newfound determination to mend their relationship, but upon entering, he found Eugenie and her mother admiring a dressing-case filled with gold given to Eugenie by Charles. Grandet, driven by greed, attempted to seize the gold, leading to a confrontation where Eugenie threatened to harm herself if he did not relent. 

In a moment of hesitation, Grandet ultimately chose to back down, allowing Eugenie to keep the dressing-case. He then attempted to show affection towards both his wife and daughter, scattering coins on the bed and promising to care for them. Despite his efforts, Madame Grandet's health continued to decline, and she passed away peacefully, leaving Eugenie to navigate a world filled with selfishness and sorrow.

In her final moments, Madame Grandet imparted a poignant message to Eugenie about the true nature of happiness, emphasizing that it lies in heaven.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
On the day after her mother’s death, Eugenie Grandet felt a renewed attachment to her childhood home, where she had endured much suffering. Her father, M. Grandet, displayed unexpected tenderness towards her, which surprised both her and the household staff. However, this behavior was soon revealed to be a façade for a more self-serving agenda.

During a family dinner, M. Grandet, with the notary Maitre Cruchot present, informed Eugenie that she was now her mother’s heiress. He pressured her to renounce her rights to her mother’s estate, claiming it would spare him from taxes and the burden of an inventory. Despite her confusion, Eugenie agreed to sign the deed, believing it would ease her father’s worries. M. Grandet promised her a monthly allowance of a hundred francs, but he never fulfilled this promise, instead giving her a few jewels as a substitute.

Over the next few years, M. Grandet trained Eugenie in managing the household and his finances, instilling in her his miserly habits. Their lives became monotonous, marked by daily routines and the absence of joy. Eugenie’s only social interactions were with the Cruchot family and their friends, who played whist with her father. As M. Grandet aged, he became increasingly frail, and when he suffered a stroke, Eugenie devoted herself to caring for him.

M. Grandet’s death was uneventful, marked by his obsession with his hidden wealth. In his final moments, he expressed concern for his treasures rather than for his daughter. After his passing, Eugenie learned from Maitre Cruchot that she inherited a vast fortune, including three hundred thousand francs in income and millions in investments and gold. Yet, her thoughts were consumed by her missing cousin, Charles.

Eugenie mourned her father and her lost love in solitude, with only Nanon, her loyal servant, to confide in. Nanon’s marriage to Antoine Cornoiller brought some joy, but Eugenie remained isolated in her grief. Despite her wealth, she felt no happiness, as her life had been overshadowed by her mother’s suffering and her own unfulfilled love for Charles.

As time passed, the Cruchots and other suitors began to surround Eugenie, eager to win her favor and wealth. They flattered her, but she remained indifferent, still longing for Charles. The Cruchots, led by M. de Bonfons, sought to position themselves favorably in her eyes, but their efforts felt hollow to her. 

Madame des Grassins attempted to persuade Eugenie to consider marrying the Marquis de Froidfond, but Eugenie’s heart remained with Charles, who had not contacted her in seven years. The contrast between her wealth and her emotional desolation deepened, leaving her trapped in a life devoid of love and joy.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
While events unfolded in Saumur, Charles Grandet was amassing wealth in the Indies. His initial success yielded six thousand dollars, and he quickly learned that buying and selling people was the most lucrative business. He ventured to Africa, purchasing enslaved individuals and combining this trade with other profitable ventures. Driven by ambition, he aimed to return to Paris with a substantial fortune and reclaim a more prestigious position than before.

As he navigated various cultures, Charles's moral compass shifted; he became skeptical and hardened, losing sight of right and wrong. He engaged in various exploitative practices, including usury and smuggling, and even bought stolen goods at low prices to sell for profit. Initially inspired by his cousin Eugenie, his memories of her faded as he indulged in a life of excess and debauchery, ultimately severing ties with his family.

In the Indies, he adopted the alias "Shepherd" to protect his identity while pursuing wealth through unscrupulous means. By 1827, he returned to Bordeaux aboard the "Marie Caroline," bringing with him gold-dust worth nearly two million francs. On the ship, he met Monsieur d’Aubrion, a man whose financial struggles stemmed from marrying into a fashionable family. The d’Aubrions were now desperate to secure a wealthy son-in-law, and Madame d’Aubrion saw potential in Charles.

Upon arriving in Paris, Charles became close to the d’Aubrion family, who sought to marry him to their unattractive daughter. Madame d’Aubrion promised to help him gain a noble title and a lucrative position at court, which excited Charles. He envisioned a future filled with wealth and status, overshadowing any thoughts of Eugenie.

Meanwhile, Eugenie remained in Saumur, reflecting on her past with Charles. One day, she received a letter from him, which she initially struggled to open. The letter revealed that Charles had returned wealthy but had moved on from their youthful love. He expressed that he was free and had been proposed a marriage to Mademoiselle d’Aubrion, which would secure his social standing. He acknowledged their past but emphasized that love in marriage was a delusion, and he prioritized his ambitions over their childhood promises.
